THE RED PAGE.
Death in Life .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is the Night that weeps—it is not I.
The ram flashed >on my pallid cheek, and seemed
Like mine own tears; but weeping-time’s gone by:
Dry-eyed I wake from the sweet dream I
dreamed.
It is the Wind that sighs—it is not I.
Though low I crouch with ashes on my head,
My breath comes slow—too cold to moan or sigh :
The living only grieve, and not the dead.
It is the Sea that sobs—it is not I
Wailing and sobbing on the misty shore,
Like some wild living thing in agony
That strains to unreached Joy for evermore.
Alas ! tis only Life that weeps and sighs—
Life with quick feaus and quenchless hope still
fed !
Death cannot hope or fear, or ever rise
From out its thrice-sealed grave—and I am
dead 1
B.E.H.
The Mew from Brummagem.
And they crucified him, and parted his gar-
merits, casting lots.” Parkes’s literary garments
nave been parted among several hands; and it
looks as if the oruoifixion did not precede—is still
to follow. “An Emigrant’s Home Letters, by
Henry Parkes, with preface and notes by Annie
T. Parkes ” (Angus and Robertson) is first in the
field of posthumous literature, and it rudely
tears away most of the few rags of personal
reputation that remained to the old man
from Birmingham. Miss Parkes (his daughter)
oif ’ n? f C c ou £ s ? J , n ,° conception of this. Like
all other of Parkes s female relatives—like nearly
a 1 women with whom he came in contact-
she appears to have been thoroughly dominated
by his personality, thoroughly deceived by his
professions and posturings. .Her literary attitude
is that of a votary at a shrine : she is gushful, and
feminine, and overwhelmed by the honor of her
parentage I send forth these pages,” she says,
to tell their simple story of how, in spite of
outer poverty, much hardship, and utter want of
education, Henry Parkes fought his way to ever-
lastnig fame.’ “The mistakes, the errors, and
the failures of the last years of his life will be
wnped away as one would brush dust from a be-
loved dead face. Who that is just will judge of
wu- u 7 • bet ween seventy and eighty?”
Which is filially magnificent, but by no means
sense. As applied to such a career as Parkes’s
perhaps to any career—“everlasting fame” ha
no meaning. What perpetuity does this ridicu
lous planet, in the revolutions of a score of thou
sand years, reserve for the memory of the verv
greatest of her sons? Nor can a truthful bio
grapher choose any part of sis subject’? life aud
say: Forget all the rest; judge sim by this.’
Parkes must be judged by his whole record, his tota
result, and there are many who, even in the ore
sent when Ins deeds bulk large, find bis career on
of high potencies, of great opportunities, and o :
comparatively poor and ignoble performance.
* * * *
Parkes spent his youth and early manhood in and
about Birmingham—“old Brummagen,” as h
calls it. He was married in 1836, left Binning
ham for London late in 1838, London for Aus
tralia in March, 1839. His daughter’s book re
prints 31 letters, chiefly addressed to his aides
sister Sarah, written during his stay in Lon
don and up to four years after he reached
Australia. There are also two letters written by
his first wife, Clarinda Varney ; and an appendix
of two sets of valueless verses by Parkes, and
letter from J G. Hornblower, a youthful com
panion of sent m response to his daugh
ters request for information concerning his earlier
years. Brother Hornblower, however, does not
blow for Parkes to any great extent, though he
mentions that P. put him to use (characteristi-
cally) to print some verses in many an hour of
overtime, unknown to his employer. And Brother
Hornblower concludes that he “ shall always feel
a pride in having been associated with one who has
■ ' W £ n l or himself th e most coveted honor
iungland s Queen can bestow ” —which is Greek
pure and simple.
* * * *
Parkes’s letters present him as a heartless ego-
tist, prodigal of fine language and insincere senti-
“Tr' u t They i - ha X e no Bterary value, but give
sidelights on his character which go to make more
vI i V J u HE Bulletin’s conception of him as a
selfish poser-ruthlessly selfish, posing continu-
ously The sty.e is like the man-alwavs
grandiose never grand. Apart from its personal
interest, the. matter has little or none. Parkes’s
chmf theme was himself and his wants. Only
, n hero-worship could have assaulted
the public with trivialities like “You had better
put the brown paper package in the rabbit-pen,
and be sure that the boards are nailed down on
e ye n such passages give a glimpse of
the real Parkes. He was as full of advice as he
was empty of cash. In his second letter he tells
now lam in debt to Mr. Porter ” —a Birming-
ham surgeon “and have been so for a shameful
longtime.” “I owe,” at the very outset of his
career- I owe” at its ending. They were the
Alpha and Omega of Parkes, those letters I O,
standing for egotism and improvidence the
whole man lay between them. Apparently “ Mr.
Porter ” went unpaid to the end.
Parkes s name and fame were built on poor
sacrificed women’s hearts and lives. His selfish-
ness was invincible, brutal. Wretched indeed
household which he left in Birmingham
in I©3B. Hi aged father was sick and often out
of work; his aged mother an invalid ; the hard
toil of his sisters kept the home together. When
Paikes conceived the idea of emigration, he was
apparently too impatient to save a small fund to
pay his expenses. A fortnight or so after reaching
Bondon he had only 4d. left. His sisters stinted
themselves to send him money, tools, and such
articles as they thought would be useful to him :
and various poor relatives were laid under con-
tribution for a few shillings to help the emigrants.
Parkes took all cheerfully, and repaid the poor
creatures in Grand isonian blessings and promises
to repay when he got to Australia. How they
lose u “ Im and slaved for him ! On Deo. 7,
1000, he acknowledges four half-crowns, and a
parcel of stuff, and “ was quite overpowered with
the sense of your kindness.” On December 31st
he acknowledges another parcel, and bemoans his
distresses.
But believe me, I do not tell you of my misery to make
jou miserable. Oh no ! I hope you will not be more un-
PW on my account than if I was living in the most
perfect prosperity, though I wish your sympathy in
believing I do not deserve all I suffer.
The fellow is never done posing—or begging. “If
Booker would make my shoes,” he writes on Jan.
IBt, 1839, to poor Sarah, “and you think you
could pay him in two months after I am gone, I
should be very glad to have them.” It is I—l—l
throughout the letters, with perfunctory refer-
ences to Clarinda, and formal protestations of
solicitude. But there is not a heartcry in all the
catalogue of his carefully polished phrases.
Glarinda s unpremeditated affection puts him to
shame. My heart feels bursting with gratitude
to you all, she writes. “ Once more I thank you
tor your unspeakable kindness.” Parkes weeps
with one eye only ; the other is always glued to
the mam chance.
* * * *
But he means to repay all the kindness shown
him. On March Ist he writes : “If I get a situa-
tion at *.50 or £6O a year and a house to live in, I
shall be able to send some money heme in a year
and a-half from the present time, and enough
soon to pay all IO in Birmingham. And that,”
says the humorous dog (though perhaps he meant
it, or convinced himself he meant it) “will be a
happy time with me.” Anon he bursts into
ecstasies of largiloquenb emotion :
mother ' my fond, affectionate
parents. God Almighty bless them, and provide for their
few remaining years better than I can tiope they will be
they be daily surprised with eom-
torts, and may floods of unexpected joy continually
descend into their hearts, &c., &c. J
This is very rich, considering that this devoted
son was ab that moment consulting his own ends
b y emigrating, leaving his old parents to be sup-
ported by his sisters. But Parkes is an incurable
phrase-maker—a sorb of Complete Letter-Writer
—with a Johnsonian sentence pat for every occa-
sion. And he was proud of ’em, too ;he gave his
consent to the publication of these letters; and
Miss Parkes is quite taken in, and praises their
style as beautiful.” Doubtless the simple
women who received them were taken in too.
* * * *

On Jan. 26th, 1843, Parkes hears of the death
or his mother, and rises to the occasion in a
beautiful letter of empty phrases. The style
Tw-n fa J«ili a r. It is kin to the style
of Wilkins Mxcawber, “piling up big words,”
self ” 1 a^ec k still intensely enjoying him-
When I knew for a certainty that I should see that
dearest face no more-that she, whose love for us
gathered strength from affliction and misfortune was
now testing in the grave and could no longer participate
in her children s happiness or sorrow, when I knew that
my mother indeed was dead, I felt as if a portion of this
world’s beauty was lost to me for ever. P J
The well-fed, “comfortable” Parkes, sur-
rounded by his collection of the best authors,
complacently calculating his periods over his
mother s cofhn-che mother who had been stinted

mother to whom, three-and-a-half years after his

stiver—not even in repayment of the ad vances for
his outfit screwed out of his sisters’ miserable
earnings!—what a spectacle ! Sister Sarah her-

chus found two snakes in his bed, one male ant

Ihey told him if he killed the male one he woulc

first, so the generous old chap knocked the

Zadig : ‘lt was noted at Poverty Point re-
cently that HenriMurger’scharmingbook, “Scenes
de la Vie de Boheme,” has been dramatised in
America, where it has succeeded “Trilby” in
point of popularity. The play should thoroughly
deserve its success, for, though the book lacks
great central figures like Trilby and Svengali, its
characters are more human, and from cover to
cover it breathes an aroma of the vagabond life of
poverty and plenty, constancy and fickleness, and
all the various vicissitudes that have made their
youth in the Q&artiW Latin an affectionate re-
membrance for so many. And, unlike “Trilby”
it is never tiresome. It has no pages of “ author”
to be skipped ; no stupid epilogue ; and the pathos
is never lengthened to tedium. For it is pathetic.
Where is there anything more touching than the
beautiful little tale, “Le Manchon de Francine ”?
or, to go to the other extreme, so humorous as the
first sketch in the book, “Comment fut institne
Je cenacle, or the story of Ali Rodolphe, whose
address is given as “ Avenue de Saint-Cloud, dans
le troisieme arbre & gauche en sortant du bois de
Boulogne, sur la cinqui&me branche ” ? ’
; ** ow about the permanence of
Trilby ’ ? Always shy of falling in with a
craze, I yet think this the best English novel
since Adam Bede,’ or I will let down a peg and
say Ihe Moonstone.’ Most of its vogue is of
course, factitious. We will not -insult Dickens,
lhackeray, or Scott. Yet, intrinsically, and
bearing m mind what it only claims to be,
Xrilby is first-class Frenchy literary work. I
have not heard, though, whether it excites the
admiration of Bourgeb, Daudet, and the other
masters, bub I fancy they are warned off and
biassed by the stupidity of the quite unreasonable
T Trilby ’ naturally stinks in the nostrils
of the London criticasters for this reason. The
adventitious has prejudicially gathered around
and clouded it, as with ‘Lothair, ’ not so clever a
book.’

Medical.
COUGHS, COLDS ON THE CHEST, BRON-
CHITIS AND ASTHMA RAPIDLY
CURED BY Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure.
Those who have taken this medicine are amazed
at its wonderful influence. Sufferers from any
form of bronchitis, distressing cough, difficulty of
breathing, hoarseness, pain or soreness in the
chest, experience'delightful and immediate relief,
and to those who are subject to colds on the chest
it is invaluable, as it effects a complete cure. It
is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.
“Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir,— Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered
for upwards of seven years, during; which period I was
scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the diffi-
culty of breathing was so distressing that for nights in
succession I had to sit up. X write you this acknowledg-
ment from a sense of duty, as in my case every other
treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much worse, and at the time I obtained your
medicine I was confined to bed suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE HOME-COMING.
Bail up, old Roany !
Quick ! For the sun is low.
Then to the creek bed stony,
Then to the lucerne go.
See, all the ridges glow ;
The white moon begins to show ;
And through the scrub my Tony
Jogs briskly along, I know.
High on Drumrossum
He boiled his quart mid-day;
A cliff, white with blossom,
Showed me a cloud of grey.
What lots he will have to say—
He’s been such an age away;
And Andy, my little possum,
Dad’s coming home to stay.
Run, little Andy!
That is his whip I hear.
Tanned, and saddle-bandy,
Comes home your Daddy, dear.
Run, for he must be near;
Let down the rails for cheer.
Show him how strong and handy
You’ve grown in the last half year.
Sorrow is over.
Oh, how my heart beats free !
Here comes my drover —
With love for his Poss and me.
That was the old coo-ee-e ?
There ! Yes ! It’s he ! It’s he !!
Home comes my dear old lover,
How happy my life will be !
FFOLLIOTT.
MY TWO MATES.
Last trip my mate
Was bronzed and rough,
Stalwart; and straight,
And his voice was gruff;
He was not a beauty for looks,
But his heart was of sterling stuff.
Now, for all my trips
Shall my mate be fair ;
She has laughing lips,
She has shining hair,
She has eyes that are tender- and true,
Her voice is beyond compare,
And her heart is a womanly heart
Life’s troubles or joys to share.
My old mate has gone back to the West —
As a “ hatter ” henceforth he may roam ;
I shall follow his footsteps no more :
With my new mate I’m staying at home.
W.A.
CURLEW.
OWNER GOING WEST.
tSaRM tor sale ; a bargain ; owner going West. Apply
-E in person to John Muokm, Coffee Palace.
“ Twenty miles this side of Hay—
Rich in pasture, fat with sheep—
S’leetion that will always pay,
S’lection mortgageless and cheap ;
Rooted, cleared, and fenced around,
Sheds and stables all complete ;
Better squattin’ can’t be found
Than the farm at Gurrundeete.
“ Bestest soil for growin crops,
Rippin’ clover for the cows j
Juicy grass and thistle-tops,
Easy earth for decent ploughs ;
Not a rabbit you can trace,
Not a fox within a mile,
And I’ll throw in with the place
Splendid Ghost of Reuben Pyle.
“ ’Tween the bfankets (ain’t no sheet),
After graft in soakin’ grass
Icy cold about the feet,
Moon a-lookin’ through the glass.
Twelve ! the door creaks open wide :
Somethin’ white comes stalkin’ in,
White hands gropin’ at its side,
Hollow face, Hell’s eyes, sunk chin.
“ Blood is on its face of woe,
Bloody stains are on its gown ;
Blood is on its breast below,
Blood from fingers drippin’ down.
Plucky bloke wot doubts ’is eyes,
Thinks ’e dreams an’ tries to smile —
Better mind ! there ain’t no flies
On the Ghost of Reuben Pyle.
“ Want ter sell out soon as poss., i
Clear out for the Golden West;
Here’s a chance, so buy up, boss,
Say it’s done an’ let it rest.”
“ Thank yer, yes, I likes yer place ;
But, excuse me, I should smile !
Don’t care for that thrown-in case
Of the Ghost of Reuben Pyle.
Hawthorn.
HUGH R. M'CRAE.
The question is being raised in Victoria as
to whether fortune-tellers, occupying mansions
and having big bank-balances, can be prosecuted
under the Police Offences Act as “ rogues and
vagabonds.” In England they probably could,
but certain words expressly referring to fortune-
tellers in the English act are omitted in the Vic-
torian.

‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 ivords, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 31, 1896.
The Victorian State Bank.
The State Bank Bill just introduced by the
Victorian Government and doomed, almost
as a matter of course, to be slaughtered later
on by the House of 48 Rich Landlords, is
another step in a weary political pilgrimage
to nowhere in particular. The Turner
Ministry has a big majority in the Lower
House, and a one-sided mandate from the
people to effect many much-needed reforms.
It really effects hardly any of them, because
the same people who gave it a strong demo-
cratic majority in the Assembly persistently,
through sheer stagnation and hopelessness
and want of interest, allow a solid Tory
phalanx to occupy the Legislative Council,
therefore one House has an active mandate
to do many things and the other a passive
one to leave them undone. The efforts of
the Govt, to pass progressive measures, under
conditions like these, are about as useless as
an attempt to dig holes in the sea, but with
a patience that passes all language it toils
along, hopelessly endeavoring to fulfil its
promises to the country which has left it so
miserably in the lurch. It spends as much
time and labor over perfecting the schemes
of reform that are certain to be dropped use-
lessly in the Council’s waste-paper basket on
arrival, as if they really had a fair chance of
becoming law, and carries water uphill in its
sieve with a laborious persistence that is
worthy of a better cause. And when it has
passed through the Assembly all the
great measures in its programme, and
the House of Landlords has squashed
them one by one, and there is nothing to
show after years of the hardest work which
any Victorian Government has yet attempted,
it will probably drop out of sight, and his-
tory will report that it was a failure because
it failed to do the things that the community
forced it to leave undone. Democratic legis-
lation in the Victorian Assembly nowadays
only serves two purposes : It affords the
Ministry the satisfaction of knowing that it
has done its duty so far as its power extends,
and thereby lifts a load off its own political
chest; and it compels the Council to take
the responsibility of publicly rejecting every
reform that the country most wants. And
■vhen the Council has done this four or five
hundred times, it is just possible that the
advertisement thus acquired will make it
smell so badly that Victoria will rise up and
fatally sand-bag that obstructive institution.
On the other hand, it is just equally possible
that Victoria will leave it alone as heretofore.
. * * •
The Turner Government’s State Bank
scheme is the most complete and perfect
thing of the kind yet proposed in these pro-
vinces. It contains all the best features of
the Maoriland and South Australian State
Banks and also of the proposed N.S.M. in-
stitution, and it tackles all the debateable
points of the question as boldly as if the
Ministry had a reliable majority in both
Chambers and its will was practically law.
Nothing appears to be left out merely as a
concession to Fat Man sentiment ; the pur-
pose seems to have been to construct the
most perfect scheme possible, to let the
Council reject it if it felt so inclined, and then
to let that institution and the nation which
created it take the responsibility of the
rejection. It provides, in the first place,
that the Post-office and the Commissioners’
Savings Banks shall be amalgamated, the
State guaranteeing all deposits and taking
over the management. The savings of the
people are to be invested, partly in mortgages
and partly in State and municipal securities,
and the system by which impecunious
Treasurers borrow them surreptitiously to
make good the deficit is to be put an end to.
The savings department will be one of four
separate branches into which the institution
is divided. The note-department is to be the
sole issuer of paper money in Victoria, and
the policy whereby a very large proportion
of the currency consists of the unsupported
lOU’s of almost anybody who chooses to go
into the business will be wholly abolished.
The State will guarantee payment of every
note in gold on presentation at the bank’s
head-office, and a reserve of gold and State
bonds will be held to fully cover the issue.
In case of another crisis the Government in-
stitution has power to issue State notes for a
practically unlimited amount to any private
bank, providing it deposits one-quarter of
their value in gold and three-quarters in
other approved securities, and in case the
securities thus pawned are insufficient
the State has a first lien on all the other
assets. This means that, in case of a sudden
run, the private bank has an institution at
hand which will advance money on all or any
of its tangible assets, and thereby turn them
all into liquid securities at once. Such a pro-
vision as this, in the ’93 crisis, would have
saved every Vic. bank, except perhaps the
Mercantile, Federal and City of Melbourne
which were dry-rotted past redemption, from
the disaster which overtook them, and the
great slump, and the burst, and the recon-
struction, and the ruinous 4J per cent, lia-
bility, and the awful depreciation of property,
and the locking-up of almost all the country’s
loose cash, and the widespread liquidation
and industrial paralysis, and at least half the
depression might have been averted. One
notable feature in the scheme is the proposal
to issue ten-shilling notes. This will save
much wear and tear of the coinage, which is
an expensive matter to replace, and looks
like an experiment that is worth trying.
• * ♦ *
The ordinary banking department of the
institution will keep the current account of
the Government, and of anyone else who
desires to put his cash in a place where it
will never be reconstructed or suspended or
liquidated or returned after much delay in
the shape of 7s. 6d. in the £. The surplus
moneys which it is not necessary to keep on
hand to meet actual requirements will be in-
vested in State and municipal securities
instead of being buried in long mortgages or
lent to the directors. The State Bank will
have a branch in London to manage the
public debt, thereby saving the heavy charges
now paid for that purpose. This accounts
for three departments. The fourth, and
probably the most important of all, is
to constitute a State Land Bank. It
will raise money by the sale, within the
province only, of bonds bearing not
more than 3J per cent, interest, and lend
on the security of country lands at per
cent. Money is to be advanced only on land
in actual use and yielding income ; advances
are limited to two-thirds the value, and, as
they are repayable by instalments, the
margin of security will be always increasing.
Also, as a guarantee against an accumulation
of arrears, any property on which the pay-
ments are two years in arrear is to be com-
pulsorily forfeited and realised upon. One-
half the surplus profits goes to a reserve fund
to provide for possible depreciation ; the
other half goes as a bonus to borrowers, con-
sequently, with inexpensive management, it
is just possible that the State Land Bank
will really provide money at 4j per cent.,
and the value of this boon to the farmer and
pastoralist who now pay 7 to 10 per cent,
will be something enormous.
* * • •
The management is hedged round with
elaborate precautions. Among other things,
it is provided that no bank or building
society director or officer is to have a hand
in the management of the State Bank, also
no uncertificated insolvent, or person who
has compounded with his creditors, or who
has been convicted of felony. The institu-
tion will be the receptacle for all unclaimed
moneys lying in private banks, and these, if
still unclaimed, will become the property of
the State. As in Canada, the private banks
will be required to hold 40 per cent, of their
local reserves in Government notes, which
will effectually prevent any combination
among them to keep State paper out of cir-
culation. The State Bank is to have branches
throughout the country, so that it will be a
national, and not merely a metropolitan, in-
stitution, and it will provide cheap money
for municipalities as well as for private bor-
rowers by making advances within certain
prescribed limits at a much lower rate oi in-
terest than the average country municipality
now has to pay. In fact, except for the one
matter that the bill does not at present give
power to lend money on town and city pro-
perty, it seems to deal with every possible
feature of the question, and if the /Victorian
State Bank should, by any possible chance
come into existence on the proposed hues, it
promises to be the most complete and best-
devised institution of its kind on earth.
* * * *
The chances for and against the success of
the measure are comparatively easy to sum
up. It will almost certainly be _ well-sup-
ported in the Assembly, which is mainly
democratic, and where the Turner Ministry
has vast influence. It will also be supported
by the agricultural and pastoral population
and by the owners of agricultural and pas-
toral lands, who want cheap money and who
are now charged, by banks and mortgage
companies and private usurers, far heavier
rates than city borrowers. Further, it will
be endorsed by investors who regard the
bonds of the Land Bank Department as a
safe and convenient investment, by some
business people who want to escape further
reconstruction of their balances, by country
municipal authorities who see a prospect of
cheaper loans than they can procure under
present conditions, by many people who are
not directly interested merely for the sake of
democratic principle and out of regard for
the best Ministry which Victoria has yet
possessed, and probably by those few mem-
bers of the Council who are not bank share-
holders or under the control of the banks,
and who have large areas of country lands
which the cheap-money scheme will materi-
ally benefit. On the other hand, it will be
bitterly opposed by the banks, their share-
holders, and all the people on whom they
can bring sufficient pressure, by finan-
ciers, loan and mortgage companies and
miscellaneous usurers, and by the Tory
party in general, excepting only that section
of it which would be directly benefited ; and
it will probably be viewed with comparative
indifference by a large section of the town
and city population, because they will gain
no immediate direct advantage, and will
probably fail to realise that the Land Bank
principle, if successfully applied in the
country, must ultimately be extended
throughout the province. In the Assembly
the success of the measure is almost certain.
In the Council its prospects depend almost
wholly on the relative strength of the country
landed interest on one hand, and the
banking, financial and city property interest
on the other, and also on the question
whether, even if the former should prove
stronger than is generally supposed, the
Upper House has not contracted a habit of
being against the Turner Government which
is now practically incurable. Among all
these chances the prospects of the measure in
the House of 48 Rich Landlords are far from
bright. Probably Premier Turner has little
hope of any good results just at present, and
merely goes on with his scheme out of his
laborous habit of doing his duty to the utter-
most, even though the heavens should fall.
And, so far, the heavens, whose name is
mostly Sargood, generally have fallen on
the indomitable Turner, and scattered his
carefully devised plans to the winds.
Why not Gamble?
There is no Democratic objection to gambling
as an amusement. There is no objection to
land occupation. There is objection to
monopoly. As betting is conducted in Aus-
tralia, there is a heavy impost levied on wagers
by the bookmakers, i.e., the prices are shorter
than they would be on a system of co-operative
gambling such as the tote. Life and Fire
Assurance companies, Stock Exchanges and
bookmakers are gambling agents who reap
enormous profits, or spend enormous amounts
in expenses, out of the money “ invested ” with
them. Insurance is a good thing—and Stock
Exchanges may serve a useful purpose. Bet-
ting on horses, as an amusement, affords
a great deal of pleasure to thousands
of people. Stockbrokers and bookmakers
bear relation to gambling, in analogy to the re-
lation of landlords to land. In the land drama
the landlord is the villain of the piece. In the
gambling drama, behold the bookmaker and
liis class. In the liquor trade, adulteration,
slow poison, tied houses, clumsy incidence of
taxation, and private enterprise reduce “ the
trade ” to a mere tout for the Devil of drunken-
ness. The supply aggravates the demand, in-
stead of the demand regulating the supply.
The State should be its own pub.-keeper, its
own land-monopolist, and its own bookmaker.
The tote doesn’t give credit, doesn’t stiffen
horses, doesn’t go insolvent, and doesn’t make
unfair wagers. Best of all, the tote doesn’t
tout for business.
* * ♦ *
Victoria, during the past 12 months, has had
several instances of pious persons and Chris-
tians in the worst sense of the term get ting up
to denounce gambling. The Leg. Council
(which is excessively pious) had the Streets
Betting Suppression Bill before it for six
weeks, and spent days in prayerful considera-
tion of its clauses, lest by chance Caulfield
racecourse (which is near a street) should be
accidentally included in its provisions. Soli-
citor-General Cuthbert’s speech in advocating
the measure contained only one argument, viz. ,
that many young fellows stole postage-stamps
from their employers and used the proceeds in
betting. A Supreme Court Judge, lecturing at
a “ Pleasant ” Sunday Afternoon, said that
wagering a shilling was analogous to two per-
sons deciding by lot which of the two should
make a small wound in the other with a knife.
On the other hand, a game of tennis for love
left a noble exhilaration to the victor without
pain to the loser. Then Rev. Marshall, of
Scots’ Church, has a word to say :
“ Nor would anyone but the most uncompromising
Puritan condemn as a deadly sin the little friendly wager,
which is but a counter or souvenir of one’s interest in the
day.”
Then he goes on to condemn Cup-day, as a day
“ Memorable for life-long sorrows and regrets, for
blasted hopes, for ruined reputations, for suicidal despair.”
Last November also, one Meiklejohn howled
in the Presbyterian Assembly :
“ An essential element in gambling is the selfishness of
the motive, and that is absent in the case of church
raffles. Raffling is more like a subscription sale than like
betting or sweeps. The element of chance is present, but
that is not the main element.
A Castlemaihe (Vic.) parson, who objected to
raffles, introduced an e' asion of the principle
to his bazaars. He dec ee i that all buyers of
tickets shall get a prize—but there is one prin-
cipal prize to be “ raffled for.” Everybody else
gets a cheap lolly wrapped in tissue paper.
* * * *
These instances show how the pious person
argues the subject of gambling, and how he
evades the issue. That historical tornado,
Talmage, once preached a splendid sermon on
life-assurance, indicating that a Christian should
not only have his soul saved but his life
assured. What is a life-policy but a tote-
ticket ? Fire insurance is a more flagrant case,
and marine insurance is one of the biggest
gambles in the world. London Lloyd’s is only
a common gaming-house, legalised. There they
will insure a race-meeting against rain. They
will insure a man against his wife having twins.
They will even insure a collier leaving New-
castle, N.S.W.
* * * *
The conclusion of the matter ? Vide “Sport-
ing Notions.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Those “ Disastrous Effects.”
The man who writes the Sydney Telegraph
articles on the “ Disastrous Effects of Protec-
tion in Victoria ” has a truly fearsome capacity
for giving himself and his cause away, and
when he has done this rather more violently
than usual, he raises three cheers and says that
his argument is now conclusively established.
In the last Saturday’s instalment of his tale of
woe he advanced two sets of figures, both of
which, by his own showing, severely bashed
Freetrade on the head, and he proudly asked
the community to look at them and see how
clear the whole affair was. The first one read
as follows :
There are two phenomena by which the comparative
material condition of the people of these colonies can be
measured more accurately than by any other. The one is
the marriage and birth rate, the other is the consumption,
per head of the population, of the necessaries and simple
luxuries of life. In Great Britain, as well as in these
colonies, it has been found that every improvement in the
condition of the masses of the people is at once followed
by an increase in the rate of marriages and births, and by
an increase in consumption; that every disturbance of
trade and industry resulting in scarcity of employment
and lower wages, is at once reflected by a reduction in
both these respects. If then, we find that in one colony
the birth and marriage rates and also the rate of con-
sumption are uniformly slower than in the other, we are
quite certain that the material condition of its people is
worse, and the difference between the rates gives us at
least an approximate measure of how much it is worse.
MARRIAGE AND BIRTH RATE PER 1000 OF POPU-
LATION.
The table is perfectly correct; and it proves
exactly the opposite of what the Telegraph’s
maddened correspondent alleges. Up to 1871
both provinces followed Freetrade, therefore
the fact that the Victorian marriage rate
was 1*39 and its birth rate 3"28 per 1000
behind that of N.S. Wales has nothing to
do with the fiscal controversy. Then Vic-
toria went in for Protection, and now its
marriage-rate is only .27 and its birth-rate 2.10
behind. In other words, the gap has lessened
immensely, and Victoria under Protection isn’t
nearly so far behind as was the same province
under Freetrade. The rates have diminished
in both provinces—but in the Freetrade pro-
vince they have diminished almost twice as
fast. Having thus put both feet in his mouth,
the Telegraph man proceeds to show that the
country which consumes most victuals per head
of population is the most prosperous, and heaves
this other "table at the head of a distracted
community :
CONSUMPTION OF FOOD, 1894.
This adds up to show that the Cornstalk gets
through 10311 b. of provisions per annum, while
the Victorian uses 10251 b., but if the two
articles—salt and tobacco—which are hardly
solid victuals are left out, the Victorian is
distinctly ahead. But the Telegraph man
doesn’t add them up. He simply remarks
that :
These two facts, the higher rate of consumption and of
marriages and births, show to demonstration that the
protective policy of Victoria has been a curse to its people ;
that it has wrecked the lives and the prospects of thou-
sands of human beings, condemning them to a state of
celibacy, and depriving them of the joys and elevating


influences of family life, and that it has carried want and
deprivation into innumerable homes.
Great Scott ! ! 1
*
The National Ass in Reply.
Mr. William Epps, secretary of the N.S.W.
Ass, writes as follows :
In a recent issue you criticised at some length the an-
tagonistic attitude taken by Liberty, the journal of the
Australasian National League (N.S.W. section), upon the
old-age pension scheme of the N.S.W. Parliamentary
committee. Permit me to say that your article is one of
the most remarkable pieces of logic I have seen —even in
The Bulletin. Apparently you have come at last to
recognise that there is something in “ thrift ” —at which
you have so frequently railed —and which you explain
“ consists largely in the individual putting away money
to support him in his old age.” The chief merit of the
scheme, in your eyes, appears to be that this thrift will be
exercised by individuals putting their “ savings in charge
of the State, where there seems to be reason to believe
they will be moderately safe,” and getting them back in
the form of pensions. I have looked up the scheme of
the committee as proposed, and find it to be roughly as
follows :—Every person over 60 years of age, who has been
15 years in the colony and has an income of less than £SO
a year, is to receive a pension of 10s. a week, and married
couples of 15s. a week. The money assumed to be neces-
sary for this proposal is to come from the following
sources: £35,000 from a tax on totalisators ; £BOOO
from billiard-licenses ; £6OOO from fines for drunkenness ;
£BOOO from a tax on sports and entertainments ; £22,000
from the customs taxation on wines, spirits, tobacco, &c. ;
and £II,OOO from a saving in the cost of charitable insti-
tutions. So your idea is that the best means of inculcat-
ing “thrift” is to get people to put their money into
totes, billiards, unlimited 3d. drinks (please explain
whether you mean long or short ones), athletic sports,
and drinks again, or tobacco. That is what you describe
as putting one’s “ savings in charge of the State.” Ergo,
the more you swamp in those forms of saving, the better
off you will be. May I, before concluding, suggest that
your ancient joke upon the former title of this organisa-
tion is becoming a trifle thin and threadbare. I think it
is now nearly five years old, and originally it was cribbed.
In reply to which The Bulletin cheerfully
admits that it has called the National Ass an
Ass for five years or even longer, and the name
is getting monotonous. Still it isn’t half so
monotonous as the fact that the National Ass
has been a National Ass for five years or more,
and as long as it continues to be an Ass this
paper must call it one merely for want of a
better name. For the rest, The Bulletin pre-
sumes that Thrift (not a vote for Thrift) is a
good thing—the National Ass organ has said it
so often that it is too late for it to go back on its
own statement now. And if the community
decides to tax its own luxuries—its own beer
and billiards and gambling, and other things—
in order to provide for its own old age, then it
compels itself to pay more for them and puts
away the difference, and if that isn’t the beauti-
ful virtue of Thrift in its most aggravated form
—what is it? Mr. Epps seems to hold that tax-
ing beer for a good purpose is not Thrift; does
he therefore mean that Thrift consists in not
taxing beer ?
The Pension Disease.
Among 380 existing pensioners on the Vic.
Civil Service there are 96 who draw £47,230
per annum, giving an average of £492. This is
a mild scandal compared with the state of
affairs in N.S.W., but a recent exposure of the
aforesaid figures in Vic. Assembly should en-
able the Democratic members to demand a
reasonable reconstruction of pension principles.
The Civil Service system, under which a big
salary entails a proportionately big pension
when the employb ceases to pick his teeth in
the office, and retires to pick his teeth else-
where—in England frequently—is a Fat Man
system which cannot be honestly defended.
Presuming that each fellow in receipt of a high
salary is worth it (which, as a rule, he isn’t),
and that he earns his .£IOOO a year quite as
fully as another civil servant earns his maxi-
mum reward of £3OO a year, then the
difference between the values of their
respective labors is properly recognised
during their working lives, and they should be
considered to quit the service on equal terms.
The superior brains and industry of the better
servant have achieved for him the better
chance to accumulate capital in his business at
the Government office, and when he knocks off
work at the age of 55 or 60, he deserves exactly
the same amount of pension as the £3OO a year
man who retires with him. Besides, no worker,
of any description, can show just cause for ask-
ing a handsome pension from the source which
has yielded him liberal and steadily increasing
salary for 30 years. The argument that his
high wages have “elevated him in the social
scale ” and made him an expensive person to
keep going, and that, therefore, he needs a
high pension to save him from comparative
ruin, is the very hollowest of Fat Men argu-
ments, and it is only fair to Fat Man to state
that he never supports it in practice in his own
private business. His old employes are not
requested to retire on two-thirds pay at the age
of 60 or anything of that sort. Intense grati-
tude for former services is almost peculiar to
the Civil Service and joint stock companies,
where the authorities who ordain the pensions
are dealing with other people’s coin.
Nervous Diseases Wanted, an Expert.
“ Medicus ” to The Bulletin :
While specialists in various branches of medicine and
surgery are thick as totes in Sydney, many of them eking
out a bare existence, the fact remains that there is no
really first-class man in the province who makes nervous
diseases his speciality. It was only happy chance that
Bent us a Chambers ; when will another such chance send
ns a Da Costa, a Hammond, or a Ranney ? The time is
ripe for the appearance of a really good man of experience
in the diagnosis and treatment of the most distressing
class of diseases that affect mankind. At the present
time, when a person who has evinced symptoms of some
mental affection is hauled up before the average doctor
for examination, the chances are about six in ten that, no
matter what the origin of the trouble or peculiarity of
its manifestation, the diagnosis will be “Incipient G.P.”
(general paralysis), and “ He’s in the asylum now” will be
the song of the afflicted one’s relatives thereafter. It is a
fact, however, that there are an appalling number of cases
of general paralysis in the N.S.W. asylums, the young and
well-nourished being the more frequent victims. It is
further to be noted that while whites of all ages and con-
ditions—laborers, doctors, lawyers, engineers, &c. —are
such frequent victims to this disease, yet Dr. Blaxland,
of Callan Park, asserts that in 15 years’ experience in
Govt, asvlums of all the Chinese that have come under
his observations only four have had g.p. As all Chinese
are supposed to be opium-smokers, this statement fur-
nishes food for reflection. Many of those affected by g.p.
are as happy as larks on account of the imaginary posses-
sion of wealth “ beyond the dreams of avarice,” while
many others are in direct communication with the
Throne, and are hourly receiving marked evidences
of royal favour. One assertion the writer desires
to make with a loud voice is that the custom
in vogue here of incarcerating epileptics in insane
asylums is an accursed outrage and a relio of barbarism.
It would be just as sensible to send
with toothache or a young woman with Jitusd
£ V M«*S epi'psy. The .-at -jority of
indeed, and even during the attaok the patient is unawa
of his condition simply because he is unoonscicms. n t
mad. However, an epileptic is like Proverbial Irs .
man’s flea in that you never know when you have Him,
hence relations get tired of the eternal vigilance 'pessary
for safety, and the poor devil is W “
insane asylum to fret away his Me. Far betterM
him right away. One peculiarity observed bj
doctors and attendants is the marked partiality
shown by epileptics for each others society.
On the admission of a fresh patient the old hands
afflicted with the disease make friendly overtures to him,
and for hours they wander about together, chatting in tne
most chummy manner and avoiding the other patients.
What is wanted is a separate institution with a staff
skilled by latest modern teaching in the diagnosing and
treatment of this class of cases ; and while certain MsP.
are on their feet yelling for justice for the leper, they
might well point out to this Govt, that a few thousands
spent in the above direction will be of far greater benefit
to the country than in erecting unnecessary Parliamen-
tary buildings or promoting wild-cat exhibitions. As in
all branches of medicine and surgery there have been
“ fashions ” in the treatment of epilepsy, but always the
faculty have been compelled to fall back on the old prac-
tice of using bromides —which, while unsatisfactory, is the
best treatment yet devised. This practice is followed out
here, a favorite stock prescription being a combination of
potassium bromide, magnesia sulphate, and cannabis
indica ; this latter drug being, in the writer’s opinion, of
no more value in epilepsy than two whoops in a 10-acre
paddock. However, it imparts a nice color to the mix-
ture, so in she goes. Medical men of the U.S. are follow-
ing with great interest the experiments being conducted
by Dr. H. C. Rutter, of the State Epileptic Hospital at
Gallipolis, Ohio, in the treatment of the disease, and it is
believed that the result of his investigations may greatly
alter present methods. What the writer would like to
see legalised is the use of the surgeon’s knife on the man
who, with a family history showing epilepsy, attempts to
marry and thus probably burden the State with a brood
of strumous idiots. Let us, meanwhile, have a hospital
for epileptics, and let us have it quickly.
The Jew-all Vote.
Vic. Tories have a novel substitute for one-man-
one-vote, evolved out of the turmoil when
Patterson got the unanimous foot of his coun-
try on his chest. It is the dual, jew-all vote—
made up by giving a vote for manhood and a
vote for landownership. The idea is that the
landowner has “ linked his fortunes with the
country,” and is not a floating atom —a bird of
passage. The example of the well-advertised
big landowner who skipped to Colombo and
had to be brought back under police protection
is not quoted. Nor is the case of the other
landboomers now cooling their heels in foreign
parts. It is the unfortunate victim of the land-
lord that is tied to the country, because, even
if he would, he has not the money to pay his
fare out of it. Another argument is that the
land-owner is prima-facie more able to exercise
the suffrage, and therefore ought to have double
suffrage. In a duel with swords a man with
long arms would prima-facie have an advantage
in the fight, therefore he ought to have a longer
sword than his opponent ! In every day fact,
the man of property has personal influence that
is politically as good as one or many votes to
him—and to add to his power is to grease the
fat pig. M'Kenzie has a scheme to help the
“capitalist who could not get the thrift-vote
through having no real property.” M‘Kenzie
would extend the vote to those who pay
income-tax. Under this system Jesus Christ
might get one vote (it would be pointed out,
however, that He was only a tramp, a bird of
passage, a floating atom) while Judas Iscariot
would get the jew-all vote, on account of the
thirty pieces of silver and what he embezzled
from the bag. The jew-all vote is a cynical
piece of impudence. Already the landowners
hold the Government of Vic. in an octopus-grip,
by means of the Leg. Council’s 48 Rich Land-
lords—-and the idea that the Tories will be
allowed to further entrench themselves is one of
those jokes that lie too deep for laughter.
“ Not Before Pa.”
The present N.S.W. Chief Justice, when at the
Bar, had no great reputation as a humorist;
but he did say one good thing—and let us
hope it was original. On one occasion,
when appearing before Mr. Justice Manning,
the Judge’s son was opposed to him. ■ Mr.
Darley, finding the Judge not at all inclined
to agree with his argument, remarked that he
had a difficult case to fight, as the Judge was
against him, and “ to add to his difficulties the
son was in his eyes.” The old Judge has gone
to his rest, and a new Judge sits in his seat;
but still suitors in the Equity Court often think
that the son, if not in their eyes, is at any rate
in their way. When the Judge’s - relatives
question was raised, it was usually considered to
be a hit at the family and relatives of Mr. Win-
deyer. Ex-Judge Windeyer was not the sole
or the worst offender, for he sat only a few
months of the year in Divorce, and his clan
only practised in the Divorce Court. The
Equity Court, however, sits all the year round,
and the objectionable system of a son practising
before his father and appearing on one side or
the other is there in full swing. The position
the son referred to has attained at the
Bar, to which he was admitted in 1888,
makes one suspect that his admitted merit
has not been the only factor in his
success. This undoubtedly able young gentle-
man has many advantages over the com-
mon or garden variety of barrister. It may
happen that sonny will argue before dad up to
lunch-time, go to lunch with him, and then
renew his argument at two o’clock. There
may be absolutely nothing in this, but the
public will think there is. The Equity
crowd are all “ English, quite English, you
know,” and, of course, for manners as well
as precedents, look to England. It is a great
pity they do not insist on the adoption of the
English rule forbidding sons to practise before
their fathers. The only (sitting) N.S. Wales
Judge who has done this is an Australian—Judge
Simpson.
The Bitter Cry of the British Manu-
facturer.
It is some time since The Bulletin alluded to
the circular of Bryant and May which speaks
of the £400,000 worth of foreign matches bought
by England, while workless thousands clamor
outside London factories. The Australasian
[Free] Trade Review now takes up the par-
able in the following fashion :
As far as matches are concerned, the whole question
may he disposed of in a few words. Those Britons who
can afford to buy the more expensive matches of Bryant
and May should, as a matter of charity, do so, in order
that a number of poor fellow-countrymen and women
may be kept in employment at fair wages. But, if the
methods of making matches in England cannot be
cheapened so as to meet the foreign competition, then the
industry will inevitably languish, and a more profitable
employment may be found for the labor and the capital
employed.
Firstly, the Freetrade journal asserts that
voluntary “ Protection ” should be given by the
charitable to an industry that is “ not self-
supporting” (to use the lingo of the cult).
Secondly, the industry must languish because
it is “not self-supporting”—and profitable
employment may be found for the workless
thousands. As to firstly: “Protection” is
held to be a righteous thing for charitable per-
sons—but, by hypothesis, it is a wicked thing
for a nation of “charitable persons.” As
to secondly : In the absence of “if” employ-
ment may be found, but it isn’t. The workless
thousands standing in London streets are
starving for an economic principle. “Employ-
ment may be found.” While the grass grows
the steed starves—a proverb that has got a
little more than musty since Hamlet referred
to it, and which is a hopeless answer to the
bitter cry of the British manufacturer.
The pastoral industry is almost the beginning,
middle, and end of Australia’s wealth. Ac-
cording to Coghlan’s “ Seven Colonies,” 84,000
people engaged in pastoral and dairying pur-
suits produce by means of the sheep and the
cow £46,000,000 a year, or £548 each. In
agriculture, 310,000 people produce £16,000,000
a year, or about £52 each. In the mining busi-
ness, 94,000 people dig and blast and smelt out
£13,400,000 worth of wealth per annum, or
£143 each ; and in manufactures 257,000 people
turn out something like £25,000,000 worth of
work, or about £IOO worth each per annum.
Thus the pastoral business brings into the
country about four times as much cash in pro-
portion to the number of persons engaged in it
as the very best of the other great industries.
And herein lies the secret of the prosperity of
Queensland and New South Wales. By
virtue of their good soil, and great area, and
comparative abundance of water, they
carry almost twice as much sheep and
cow in proportion to their population as
Westralia, S.A., Tasmania, or Victoria, and
the sheep and the cow are almost the only Aus-
tralian industry which does more than pay
expenses. Even mining, which is distinctly
the next best, doesn’t begin to be a patch on
the great jumbuck occupation. The fact that
the pastoral worker is hardly any, if any,
better paid than the miner doesn’t affect the
argument: the point that sticks out conspicu-
ously is that Australia is a comparatively rich
country with many Fat Men in it, not through
the genius of its inhabitants, who make a
remarkably moderate show in every other line
of business, but because it is large and empty,
and has plenty of elbow-room for the sheep and
the cow, and a climate which agrees with these
animals. And when Australia becomes thickly
inhabited, so that there isn’t nearly so much
sheep and cow in proportion to population as at
present, it promises to become a very ordinary
place indeed.
Melbourne Australasian does a fresh howl
about the person with proputty and a plural
vote who “ helped to build up the country ” :
The Assembly is asked to affirm that, for purposes of
legislation, the man who has no appreciable interest in
the stability of the colony is as good as anybody else.
“To say,” as Mr. M'Kenzie remarked on Tuesday even-
ing, “that a man who had come to this country, had
helped to build it up to its present condition—linked his
fortunes with the country so that he must sink or swim
with it —was to be placed on the same level as one who
had no interest in it except what he could get out of it—a
bird of passage —a floating atom —was absurd.”
Isn’t it about time this inverted wheeze about
the man of property being bound to the country
while the man without property can leave it at
any time, was put right end up ? Australasia
is surrounded by sea; the penniless man can’t
walk out of it, and about the lowest price at
which he can get out without walking is £ls if
he is single—something like £SO if he is married
and has a family. Not having the requisite
sum he can’t get out; he must sink or swim
with the country, and he has more interest in
its stability than any one else. The plural
vote man, if the state of the country doesn’t
please him, simply realises his wealth, takes a
bank draft and a saloon passage, and goes to
London to be a “representative Australian”
for ever afterwards. He is the real bird of
passage —the man who hasn’t a stake in the
country and doesn’t need to care a straw how it
is managed because he can, and does, get out of
it as often and as much as he pleases.
And why is there so much damp pathos about
“ the man who has come to this country and
helped to build it up ? ” Isn’t the man who was
born in the country and helped to build it
up worth a moment’s consideration ? And
what about the man who came to the country
and helped to pull it down ? Also, is there no
consideration due to the man who came to the
country or was born in it, and who found the
country falling down when he arrived, and in-
stead of building, which wasn’t wanted at the
time, devoted himself to holding up the re-
mains so as to keep them from crumbling any
worse ? Building up isn’t everything ; when
the building is shoddy the man who erects it
in the first place has a far lighter job than the
one who holds it up afterwards.
There is another outbreak of complaint re the
“ blackmailing ” practised upon unwary turtle-
doves who coo and cuddle in Hyde Park,
London. The parks, which ought to be the
most decent of “ spooning ” resorts, are infested
by creeping rascals who threaten charges of
“improper behaviour” against any unhappy
pair they can strike terror into. Newspapers
artlessly remark that the police, although con-
stantly informed of blackmail cases, do nothing
to suppress the 'evil. Not likely. The police
everywhere—in Australia as well as abroad—
are the root of all such evil; the creeping
rascals are, in many cases, their agents working
for a share of the plunder. They themselves
(that is to say, the rascally portion of them)
blackmail in person when it seems quite safe,
and, anyway, how could the threats of the
other fellow carry any weight but for official
co-operation ? A park loafer can’t take
Edwin and Angelina into custody. Ha
threatens to “ report them,” which is just
what he wouldn’t dare to do if the lurk-
ing myrmidons of the law were sternly
instructed to clear the park of black-
mailers. In fact, he wouldn’t be there at all if
the men in blue didn’t want him, neither would
prostitutes be there if they were not privately
licensed by the underpaid constables who levy
taxes on their miserable earnings. Policemen,
in the first place, don’t get enough money from
the Government to keep them free from dirty
dealings, and, in the next place, their occupa-
tion is demoralising in its Paul Pryish tenden-
cies. Upon the night - shaded endearments
which no considerate citizen would willingly
disturb, peeler Mickey is naturally disposed to
turn his bull’s-eye lantern.

That aggregation of Fatmen, Melb. Harbor
Trust, lately decided by 10 votes to 6 to
cast out Commissioner Treganowan’s motion
favoring a revision of the wages-system of its
employes. It was pointed out logically enough
that since the last revision in ’94 a number of
increases had been granted, in many instances
creating two scales of payment for the same
work. “ Either one lot of men are getting too
much or the other too little,” aptly observed
one of the members. But the majority would
have none of logic, nor would it admit that the
workman of Melb. to-day is in a better posi-
tion than he was two years ago —a fact obvious
to the most cursory observer. It simply
chucked out the resolution, and when it was
pointed out that some of its employes received
£7 per month for working hours a week,
its view of the question was neatly voiced by a
commissioner, who humanely remarked : “ If
the Trust were to dismiss all the men who are
dissatisfied with their position, it could get
hundreds of others ready to take their places,
and I think it would be a very good lesson for
them. It would teach them not to be per-
petually harassing the Trust to increase their
wages.” And of such is the kingdom of
Capital !
King O’Malley, the glorified Yankee bagman
of S.A. Assembly, has carried his Bill for the
abolition of barmaids. The new order of things
is to date from the beginning of 1899, so as to
allow time for the present race of Flossies to
die out. After that, only the Mrs. Publicaness
or the daughters, wives, or other female rela-
tions of publicans are to be allowed behind the
bar. Therefore, The Bulletin presumes that
some of the Flossies will set up on their own
account, and some will marry the publican and
qualify that way, and the rest will mostly be
the publican’s female relative. Any publican
of brains can arrange a few birth-certificates
showing that he has six younger sisters, and
get them to serve in succession ; and even if he
has only one sister, and she seems to be different
every few months, it probably won’t matter
much. The moral of the new law seems to be
that the bar is a place where girls get corrupted,
but it doesn’t matter whether a publican’s
woman-relatives are corrupted or not; also that
because the barmaid is a bad lot the publican
ought to marry her. The next 2J years will
probably be spent partly in marrying the beer-
Flossie, partly in teaching her to call the
publican “ pa,” and partly in hunting up bar-
maids who are sufficiently like the old man to
pass for some kind of relatives, and teaching
them the family history, so that the twin
daughters won’t be born too far apart and with
more than five years discrepancy in the date.
The results should be interesting.
4
Interesting slab from the speeeh-in-defence of
John Norton, tried at Sydney for alleged
sedition: —
This is not the first time the liberty of speech has been
in jeopardy in this colony. You will recollect, and I have
here a copy of the Treason Felony Act of 1868, when,
owing to the excitement of the foolish act of a drunken,
mad Irishman, in firing a shot at the Duke of Edinburgh
down at Clontarf, public opinion became excited, and
there were rumors of a conspiracy. During that panic,
which was similar to the Loyal Jubilee Demonstration of
10 years ago, this Treason Felony Act was passed. This
Act made it treason felony to refuse to drink the health
of the Queen when called upon to do so. It was passed
under the same inspiration of the so-called spirit of
l 03 r alty which is now prompting this prosecution. Clause
9 of that remarkable piece of legislation known as “31 Vic.,
No. 25, entituled an Act for the Better Security of the
Crown and Government of the United Kingdom and for
the Better Suppression and Punishment of Seditious
Practices and Attempts,” assented to March 18,1868 :
“ If any person shall use any language disrespectful to
Her Majesty the Queen, or shall factiously avow a deter-
mination to refuse to join in any loyal toast or demon-
stration in honor of Her Majesty, or shall by word or deed
express sympathy with the crime of any persons said to
or suspected to be or to have been engaged in the com-
mission of or in the attempt to commit any felony, or
shall hold out byword or deed as worthy of praise ... he
shall be liable to two years’ imprisonment with hard
labor.”
Imagine the consequences of refusing to join in a toast.
For instance, a man might go into a bar and be met by a
half-drunken man with a demand to drink the toast of
the Queen. This was the sort of thing that was possible
in this colony in 1868, and it happened that a man was
committed for trial under the very circumstances I am
pointing out. At Goulburn, a party of young men were
drinking in a public-house, and an obstreperous party-
man entered the bar and demanded that they should
drink the health of the Queen. One of the young men, in
a half-drunken condition, resented the impertinence of
this stranger, and said, “the Queen be damned ; I will
drink your mother’s health or mine, but I won’t
drink to the Queen.” Would you believe it, he
was indicted for treason - felony, and if it had
not been for a curious circunit'ance he would
have been convicted. The Hon. W. B. Dalley was re-
tained at a fee of £2OO to defend him, but, owing, I
believe, to the mercifulness of the Attorney-General of the
day, nobody had a copy of the statute when the case
came on for trial, and so the thing fell through. As soon
as Mr. Dalley appeared to defend the young man he
asked his Honor, the Judge who was presiding’ to allow
him to see a copy of the Act. The Judge did not have
one, the Crown did not have one, and no trace of one
could be found in the court. In this way the case was
abandoned. You will see from that what loyalty can come
to in this colony. That is the sort of thing that can be
done under the sentiment of loyalty.
An unreported incident of the jingo
“ Trafalgar ” meeting at Melbourne Town Hall.
Just as Gov. Brassey was acknowledging the
plaudits of the propertied and warlike classes,
agitator Fleming roared from the body of the hall
—“ Three cheers for France, Liberty, Equality,
and Fraternity ! Down with the aristocracy ! ”
Whereupon the instruments of law’norder ran out
Fleming, who -told them that he was bound to go
anyway, because he had another meeting to
attend. The fact that his presence is required
elsewhere must be very consoling to a man who
happens to have the policemen pushing him off
the premises.

The last production of the Bland Holt
season at Sydney Royal, “In Sight of St. Paul’s,”
is a disconnected and fragmentary thing, in which
nething leads to anything in particular. The
splendid staging and the dramatic force of'a strong
company, and the occasional strong situations,
save it from being what it deserves to be, and pre-
vent the audience rising unanimously and consign-
ing the author to the place that he ought to go to;
but, all the same, “ St. Paul’s ” is as disconnected
as a dynamited Czar, and scrappy to the verge of
desperation. It opens with a happy family—
father, sister,. a pretty she-cousin, and two
brothers a wicked captain, and a siren. One
brother, being infatuated with the siren, forges
his father s name on a bill which the wicked cap-
tain holds, and the other one, to save him, says he
did it. Then the latter goes away to toil in
obscurity, and nothing comes of his obscurity save
that the matter is cleared up, and he returns.
Later on, .he hunts up the bad brother, who is
still following the siren with a hang-dog look on
his face and a tall hat, and, while the two are
remonstrating, the captain shoots the bad brother
for no reason that transpires then or afterwards.
The siren kidnaps the pretty girl-cousin, and locks
her up in her den of gilded infamy, and then does
nothing special, except urge her to have a glass of
wine. There is a scene outside St. Paul’s, where
two .women and a child are looking at a flag and
talking about a dead soldier, and the elder woman
discovers that the others are her daughter-in-law
and grandchild, and they become reconciled and
go away together. These ladies aren’t in the
story, and their episode has nothing to do with
anybody. Finally, the wicked captain states that
if the worst comes he will suicide, but, when the
worst does come, he drops his bottle of poison
twice on the pavement, and is finally left leaning
against a wall unsuicided, and seemingly as fresh
as ever.
♦ * * *
There are other aggravating features in the
business. The heart of the audience goes out to
Miss Yirgie Vivienne, as a bright and kindly
lady of very, shady 'character, who shoots the
siren ; and it wants badly to know if she was
gaoled for it and had her lovely hair cut off, or
was hanged and had her snow-white shoulders
pawed by Nosey Bob, or if she reformed, or what?
None of these matters are cleared up. Also, it
wants to know what the thing is all about'in
general. The co. tackles the matter in an ener-
getic spirit; Miss Frances Ross looks her prettiest
and plays admirably as the angelic heroine, and
Miss Watson’s siren is a fine piece of work ; also
Baker, Norman, Charles Brown, and Mr. and
Mrs. Bland Holt all call for a lot of praise—still
the reason why so many of these people simply
walk over the edge of the tale and are never
again accounted for isn’t so clear as it might be.
Ihe conflagration in which the siren gets con-
siderably charred—she afterwards dies in the
hospital is a beautiful piece of combustion, and
a view of London in Act 1 is really excellent, and
these are the best things that can be said for the
drama. Otherwise it is illuminated hash, with
irrelevant people dropping in at times and making
remarks about Hingland’s greatness, and obscur-
ing the view of the sinful captain who ought to be
convulsed by strychnine in the corner, but isn’t.
Also there are sundry thrilling episodes—notably
the trial of the good brother, and the death of the
charred siren—which don’t appear at all. Some-
one simply drops in now and then and mentions
them, while a lot of things which might as well be
dropped in and mentioned are set out at full
length.
** * *
Her Majesty’s (Sydney), which is generally
more or less under a cloud, is having a vigorous
boom on the strength of a new melodrama
described as “ The Serpent’s Coil.” Said boom is
thoroughly well deserved. The horror-drama
like everything else, is worth doing well and to the
uttermost, and “ Tne Serpent’s Coil ” breaks every
record that The Bulletin knows of in the matter
of horror and blood-shriek and premeditated
butchery. It rages promiscuously in England
Italy and Pekin, and gibbers after gore all over
the globe. It is the embodiment of all the penny
dreadfuls on earth set to slow music ; also, it con-
tains about the most rampant villain who has
appeared up to date, and includes a steamboat on
the Bay of Naples and a boa-constrictor in China
and almost everything else. In the very first act
the bad man of the story induces his enemy to lean
against an electric machine, whereupon a huge
globe of fire strikes him dead with a sudden “ pff ”
and “ whoosh,” and his singed remains fall back
in a heap. I hereafter there is a burglary and an
attempted poisoning, and after that the story
branches off into new tracks of blood and bones
ai jd raw meat, and howls soulfully in the region
where the kraken and the literary squid have
their haunt. The bid man attempts to fry his
beautiful young wife alive in an oven, but after
telling her how she will linger and wail and
crackle, and how her hair will blaze up, he par-
tially relents, and doesn’t fry her after all, and
the proposed crackle never comes off. Instead
he merely leaves the shrieking girl alone with a
large green boa-constrictor. His last words as he
goes out are to the effect that she will be gradu-
ally squeezed, and the serpent will probably break
her legs a good deal before he slimes her all over
and svvallows her, and that the operation will
most likely take some time. Thereafter the play
becomes comparatively calm, and there is nothing-
in it but a Chinese riot and a blazing house, and
about two dozen miscellaneous fights, and a suicide
by the electric apparatus, and the arrest of almost
everybody with almost everybody else waving a
revolver and threatening to shoot the world in
general.
* * * *
There is no literary merit at all in the
“Serpent’s Coil,” and the company is mostly a
dead level, and a tolerably depressed level at that.
There is something to be said for Miss Daisy
Holly, who is a pretty and agile dancer, and Miss
Maud Lita is an exception to the general ordi-
nariness of things. Also William Cosgrove, as
the very worst bad man—one who could give
Sodom and Gomorrah 50 yards in 100 and
win easily—can’t be overlooked, partly because
his dialogue consists so very largely of “curse”
and “damn,” and partly because he owns the
green boa-constrictor. But, in a general way, and
apart from the scenic effects, the “Serpent’s
Coil ” travels on its blood-squashiness and its
singed corpse. Even when the bad man threatens
to pound his bride an hour after marriage, nobody
notices it, and the curses he heaps upon her, and
the way the corpse bobs up and down when he is
dragging it away after showing it to her, and
other small unpleasantnesses, pass unobserved
in the general confusion. In fact, even if the
depraved person of the story came in swinging a
freshly-amputated leg in his hand he would hardly
draw much attention, unless he whacked the leg
on the table very furiously indeed. After two acts
of the ‘ ‘ Serpent’s Coil ” the audience becomes
hardened, and is capable of looking on calmly
while boiling water is poured over its mother.
One anomaly, by the way, is that the serpent
doesn’t coil worth a cent. That bright green
animal only appears once and then it is straight.
** * *
The Nat Goodwin season at Sydney Lyceum
ended on Saturday night, when the American
comedian _ delivered his farewell speech, partly
through his nose, and said more kind things about
the people of Australia than were altogether de-
served. It was a neat little speech, and if the
orator had taken off and wiped a celluloid collar
as he spoke it would have suggested the Missouri
sheriff in every line. After hearing Nat in his
own private capacity the general impression is
that the sheriff is more like the real Goodwin than
any of his other characters. This last night was
made an American festival; the stars and stripes
pervaded the building, and nearly everybody had
an initial in the middle of his name and a ten-
dency to go out and have a cocktail in the inter-
vals. Now that Goodwin has departed the Lyceum
will suffer a week’s desolation, broken only by the
cries of an amateur opera co. which wrestles with
“ Maritana ” on Wednesday and Thursday under
Y.R. and other influential patronage, and
is sure to have big houses. On Satur-
day, however, the Potter-Bellew co. comes back
to play “She Stoops to Conquer,” and “Romeo
and Juliet ” follows later on.
** * *
The Brough Comedy Co. has moved on to
Melbourne, where all good Australians go in Cup
week, and Sydney Criterion is again deserted.
** * *
Coleman and. Alice Leamar are back at
Sydney Tivoli for the several hundredth time,
and that house is booming with the same old boom
which never seems to abandon Harry Rickards
now-a-days. The rival house, Adams’ Palace of
Varieties, is now announced to open at Xmas, and
Arthur Garner has returned with the harmless
necessary co. Whether Sydney can support two
first-class variety shows, each backed by much
cash and with two men in charge who know the
business to perfection and who mean to stay, is a
question that remains to be decided. If it can’t
then something must crack, and when it does
crack it will be a very big crack indeed.
** * *
“ The Two Orphans,” a story of woe in
six chapters, unfolds itself to fluctuating audiences
at Melb. Royal. The general tendency of this
adaptation from the Erench is so sad that Jacques,
the villain of the show, figures as “comic relief ”
and even appears to sparkle like a squib in a coal-
cellar. Jacques, under ordinary circumstances
would not be a gladsome item. He persecutes the
halt and the blind, converts his female victims
into petty larcenists, and leads his wicked old
mamma into grog-shops. All the same, he illumi-
nates the gloom, and is popular. When his
afflicted brother stabs him in the stomach, where
he was about to stow away a bullock’s heart with
fash stuffing, the gallery is glad to hear that
Jacques will recover, and live to be hanged
George Rignold, in this leading character, doesn’t
make vice attractive, but he gives it an air of
happiness. His laugh combines the ring of the
merry joy-bell with the muffled note of °the fog-
horn. In fact, he and his depraved parent are the
on y people who get any amusement out of life.
All the others are employed in seeking calamity
and putting salt on Sorrow’s tail, so “ The Two
Orphans ” is quite a suitable introduction to the
racing carnival.
* * * *
The acting at the Royal achieves a satis-
factory average, not as good as in “ Called Back ”
a while ago but good enough. The orphans, Miss
Emily Hughes and Miss Watts Phillips, although
rather lacking in youth and innocence, are other-
wise effective. Every time they are required to
rise to a melancholy occasion they rise in a natural
manner. Also Miss Lindahl, an inexperienced
actress who plays a soiled dove at a reformatory
bears herself prettily in a pathetic position ; and
Miss Bessie Vivian, as La Froehard, is a mother
ot whom any Jacques might be proud. H. W.
Diver, as the love-sick cripple, looks more like a
sham poet than a genuine knife-grinder ; never-
theless there is'sincerity in his method and an
admirable thinness about his legs. And he fights
craftily. Nobody could surpass Diver at sticking
a knife into the leading man’s digestion, or seem-
mg agreeably horrified when the weapon gets
there. Plunmer, who begins to spoil himself by a
feminine trick of blinking his pencilled eyelids at
space, is a sprightly champion of beauty in dis-
tress, and the six acts produce some of Clint’s best
scenery. On Saturday night Geor-e Rignold
scored a crowded house by distributing a bicycle,
which fell to the lot of a small boy situated near
the roof.
At the Alexandra, a revival of “ Monte
Cristo ” gave the customary pleasure to a limited
congregation last Saturday. The Bulletin feels
bound to speak softly of “ Monte Cristo ” for the
sake of old burlesque associations, and because it
is a droll entertainment when taken seriously.
One never knows where the drama will get to
within the next half-hour. It is likewise impos-
sible to guess where any given person will be the
next time he appears, and when he has appeared
next time the things that have happened to him
during the interval puzzle the understanding and
prevent a thoughtful observer from feeling tired.
Alfred Dampier is about the most consistent
feature of the struggle, He gets dragged away
to prison in the prologue, and is duly continued in
the next act, where he mentions that he has been
gnawing the stone walls of his dungeon-cell for 14
years. Soon afterwards he is thrown into a seeth-
ing ocean in a sack, and thenceforth his path in
life is paved with gold and crossed by new editions
of the people who hated him when he was younger.
All these have come into a terrible lot of money,
the same as Dampier. If they were ordinary
bloated capitalists, and if Dampier could persuade
himself to retire into private life on ten millions
sterling, the drama would probably end here. But
the fortunate gentleman from gaol prowls around,
waiting and hoping for vengeance, until his
jealous rivals of long ago have mostly died of vio-
lence at his hands. An odd one, who escapes
destruction, merely falls into the hands of the
police. Alfred Dampier, the inflexible Edmund
Holloway, Appleton, Lachlan M‘Cowan and
George Buller are creditably concerned in this
affair, but they will probably be concerned in
something different shortly.

“ Henry V. ” is to be placed upon the
extensive stage of Melb. Royal on Saturday
(31st) “in response to numerous requests, and in
commemoration of that great Battle of Agin-
court.” Probably the Hagincourt anniversary,
which was several days previous to the date fixed
for this revival, didn’t influence the Rignoldian
spirit so deeply as the numerous requesters.
There is always a moderate demand for Shake-
speare’s jingo panorama, and a Melb. generation
that would like to meet the original George Rex
has grown up since he last appeared on his milk-
white steed.
