A fearful conflict of Melbourne shows com-
mences this week. At the Princess’s they promise
another spell of “ Djin-Djin,” with a fresh cast,
and the Lumiere Cinematographe (truly a fine
draw, for the other ’graphe is not a circumstance
to it) on Derby night, when the Bijou will have a
grand re-opening under Brough management. “ A
Mother of Three ’’ is the programme appointed for
this latter interesting occasion. It will sail in
upon a wave of Sydney success, and the Bijou has
been redecorated to receive visitors.
** * *
To-night (Thursday) the Carnival of All
Nations falls due at Melb. Exhibition Building.
Something like =£lo,ooo is to be expended on the
Carnival. Each shilling admission ticket entitles
bearer to a remote chance of winning a golden
pyramid worth £6OO, or other useful article,
guaranteed genuine and saleable at the Mint.
“Art union” tickets, by the way, admit to the
show on afternoons only. People who.arrive at
night will be required to pay a shilling for what
they can see at the time, which will not include a
vision of winning a great hunk of gold in the
lottery. Eitzgerald Bros.' London Novelty Co.
under the Dome, and the same firm’s circus
under canvas in the Quadrangle* are principal
attractions. The Novelty Co. includes Mile.
Antonio, the poetic trapezist, and Mahomet, the
famous platform-rapper, and everybody else who
appeared at the same place last year, with the ex-
ception of poor Peart. That indomitable professor
is now— The Bulletin hopes and believes—diving
from a rainbow platform into the Jasper Sea.
** * *
At the Athenaeum Hall, a Yitascope,
claiming to be superior to all other scopes and
graphs of a similar character, will give a loaning
picture-show during race-week. Which somehow
reminds The Bulletin that Lady Brassey’s Eund
in add of the local Blind Asylum, is to have a
matinee-benefit at the Princess’s, on Nov. 11.
** * *
Cecil Ward’s break with the Brough
Comedy Co. recalls to mind his first appearance
on the Australian stage. It happened at Mel-
bourne Bijou in “ Held by the Enemy,” and re-
vealed the youthful-looking Cecil as an ultra-
romamtic lover with an enormous capacity for
gasping. His heavy method on that occasion, and
for a while afterwards, gave little promise of the
versatility which has made him a popular favorite.
Ward, as we first knew him, was an intelligent
inexperienced actor of the Charles Warner stamp
who might easily have settled down and grown
old in the melodrama-hero groove had he joined
say, Bland Holt’s co. Time was when the Sydney
gallery-boys showed a disposition to “poke borak ”
at the gushful young man, but a course of farcical
comedy with Brough and Boucicault developed his
sense of humor and fitted him for serio-comic
character sketches—such as the aristocratic tippler
in “ Mrs. Tanqueray.” Under B. and B. manage-
ment Cecil Ward played a fairly wide range
of parts in a more or less admirable manner
throughout the chapter. His Tom Jones and
Joseph Andrews, for instance, were very different
people to the boozy Orreyed aforementioned. The
Judge’s son in “A Village Priest” was a con-
siderable contrast to Joseph Surface, and the
weak-kneed earl in “The Amazons ” had no points
of resemblance to gallant Claudio in “ Much Ado
About Nothing.” Seriously sentimental in such
modern plays as “ Mrs. Ebbsmith ” and “ The New
Woman,’’the adaptable Ward could play the fool
joyously in farces, or adopt a bright, gentlemanly
middle course, as m “ The Magistrate,” and The
Bulletin takes this opportunity to congratulate
its old young friend, on behalf of Australia in
general, upon the all-round results of his strict
attention to business during the past 10 years.

The Exchange Hotel, Adelaide, stands just as Coppin
left it over 30 years ago, after making his fortune as
licensee. Then it was one of the best houses in Adelaide
as an hotel and restaurant; even now it does the best
bar trade.
Rignold struck a brilliant idea when he sent a company
of truculent supers dressed in soldiers’ uniforms, and
headed by Warneeke’s band, to march through Bourke-
street as an advertisement for “Tommy Atkins.” The
court, however, fined him ss. for having organised
an illegal procession. This was probably just the addi-
tional advt. that George wanted. Only it came too late.
The Court was mean enough, or cute enough, not to deal
with the case until the run of the play was over.
“A Night Out,” now being played at London Vaude-
ville—a wildly-hilarious and highly-suggestive French
adaptation—is to be produced in' Australia. If for
nothing else, writes a Bulletin correspondent, the night-
gown dance of four young maidens will bring the bald-
heads to the show. Much of it, however (the play
generally and not the dance), is flatly vulgar— which
accounts for its long run in London in the middle of the
dead season.
“ The Billy ” : Tie Mrs. Potter’s additions to Sheridan. I
recollect going to Hobart theatre to see Miln a< Antony,
in “Julius Caesar.” I had carefully read the play first.
At the end of the assassination scene' Miln, in giving the
speech beginning “ O, pardon me, thou bleeding piece of
earth,” cut it short at “ Cry havoc ! and let slip the dogs
of war,” arid ■ threw himself down, apparently fainting,
and I waited to see if the curtain would fall on him, as he
deserved. It nearly did. When I consider the actual
end of the scene, and the incongruity of Antony fainting,
when he ought to be hurrying to the forum, I kneel down
and thank Shakespeare’s ghost I’m not an actor.
Melb. Opera House pioneers by opening a cyclery for
the reception of the machines of cycling patrons.. Par-
sons states that every' theatre he visited in America was
so fitted, though in England, the birthplace of the wheel,
the play-going cyclist has still to leave his jigger against
the kerb, chancing theft or injury, or to tip a small boy,
possibly a cycle thief himself, to keep an eye on the pre-
cious instrument.

Miss Lily Maddocks, of the Melb. Lyric Club, a pretty-
little lady with a pretty' little voice, is said to be going
’Ome for vocal culture. In this instance the stereotyped

Nellie Stewart’s disappointment at the failure of Mus-
grove to build a London theatre for her, is reported
among her friends in Melbourne to be “profound.” But
London is over-theatred, and a fetching and talented
actress who is recognised queen in her own country might
very well rest content with that distinction. The London
theatrical speculators have too many of their own Nellie
Stewarts—or their equivalent—to provide queendoms for
others.
The financial success of Trebelli’s Australian tour has
led to the engagement of Albani by' the Vert Brothers.
Australia will now really listen to a genuine oratorio
soprano. Albani’s attainments extend to all departments
of her art. Whether in oratorio or in secular singing, she
is listened to as respectfully as any singer living.

to one another by blood or marriage. The strong woman
is the mother of one of the boy's and the wife of the dapper
little man, who is the brother of the other two women.
One of these is married to the taller man.
Madame Anna Ruppert, Alfred Dampier’s London
backer, who died the other day, made her fortune out of
a half-guinea face-wash which was simply' a weak solution
of corrosive sublimate, worth about a half-penny a pint.
The police ejected her from Germany, where there is a
law providing for the careful escort over the frontier of
the swindling quack.

_ George Rignold came within range of possible destruc-
tion last week. That is to say, he hired a furnished house
where there was an escape of gas, and if the escape hadn’t
been discovered until after his arrival on the scene
(instead of long before he took possession), and if the
person who made the discovery had applied a lighted
candle to the gassy' atmosphere in Rignold’s presence,
instead of opening all the windows and feeling for a
severed gas-pipe in the dark whilst the theatrical manager
was far away, then would Rignold aforesaid and the
premises in question have been biffed into atoms with a
bang, provided there was enough gas to do it.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The National Bank (Melbourne) publishes a
balance-sheet, which might be a great deal worse, yet is
only moderately good at the best. The net profit for past
six months is £11,707, as compared with four previous
half-yearly records of £10,523, £13,762, £15,126, and
£25,231. Consequently, things are better than they were
last half-year, and a great deal worse than during the
three preceding ones. During the half the National
has paid off over £1,600,000 of its reconstructed 4j per
cent, deposits, reducing the amount due to £3,265,000,
and the saving thus effected was expected to produce
rather better results. Preference shareholders get a 5 per
cent, dividend, which absorbs £7644, and, as the balance
would only run to a dividend of 10s. per cent, per annum
on ordinary capital, the ordinary shareholders are left out,
as usual. The National requires to earn fully £48,000
for the half-year before it can pay 5 per cent, all round,
and when the ordinary shareholders compare this figure
with the £11,707 actually earned, the smallness of their
present chances is sufficiently apparent. The National
has a splendid business; note circulation has increased
£4OOO in the half-year ; new deposits have increased by
£900,000 ; and cash and liquid assets reach the very lanre
total, despite the repayments of old deposits, of £2,780,000;
also, the new capital is coming in well, and the fixed
deposits actually sell above par, and the preference shares
are at a premium, and even the ordinary ones are saleable
at a big discount. With its immense reserves and the
steady expansion of business the National should soon be
able to release the remainder of its 4J per cent, deposits,
and then it will have a fair chance to do some good for
itself.
Against these bright features there are one or
two decidedly bad ones. The National lately wrote off
the last of its reserve-fund to cover losses. Owing to its
insensate desire to pay dividends it has only accumulated
since ’93, despite its comparatively large earnings, a credit
balance of £16,500. If there are any further heavy losses
to provide for they must come out of capital, and if the
smallness of the recent profit is a criterion the chances
are that there will be further losses to meet. The insti-
tution has nearly £400,000 in Government securities, and
over £600,000 in municipal debentures, and if these yield
only 31 per cent, all round they account for a lot more
than the whole net profit, so the inference is that the
banking business at the present time doesn’t pay ex-
penses. If the National would think seriously over this
state of things it would, perhaps, go a good deal
slower in the matter of dividends and be a lot the better
for it.
* * * *
The balance-sheet of the Colonial Bank (Mel-
bourne) suggests that something is very much wrong with
that luckless institution. The half-year’s profit is £2004
as compared with £2564 for previous half-year, £7BO for
the one before, and a loss of £4694 for six months ended
March, ’95. At the last-named date, however, the
Colonial was still paying 4)4 per cent, interest on its
deposits ; it now pays only 2)4, and the difference repre-
sents a saving of about £15,500 for the half-year. There-
fore, if things were as good with the bank as at March,
’95, it should have a profit of about £ll,OOO, and the
present state of things shows that in 18 months its profits
have really dropped between £BOOO and £9OOO. A very
little more falling-off would leave the Colonial unable to
pay even its reduced percentage, and when an institu-
tion gets such an immense concession and yet only yields a
return of about nine shillings per cent, per annum on
capital, its position looks simply abject. And when it is
further considered that it has looked worse with every
consecutive balance-sheet for the last two years, it doesn’t
improve the prospect. The Colonial seems to experience
an increasing difficulty in collecting calls, and the amount
unpaid is now £44,800. In a recent report it was stated
that the falling-off was due, in part at least, to the diffi-
culty in finding safe investments for the bank’s cash.
This might apply in other cases ; in the case of the Colonial
it is distinctly thin. An institution which gets the bulk
of its deposits at £2 10s. per cent, can afford to put its
surplus money into State debentures, of which there are
plenty to be got yielding £3 3s. per cent, or more, and
annex the difference, yet the Colonial doesn’t avail it-
self of this obvious resource, and has only £lOOO worth
of State securities among its assets.
The Colonial is another institution which is
to write off lost capital when the Companies Act
passes. Meanwhile it keeps strictly dark as to the
amount of its losses and the extent of the proposed
operation. If affairs don’t improve, however, it will be
necessary to write off all the ordinary capital (£562,000)
and practically “ chuck out ” the original shareholders,
and hand over the whole concern to the preference crowd
in order to make the profit run to even a 1£ per cent,
dividend. In fact it wouldn’t quite run to it even then.
And if it is proposed to put the Colonial on a 4 per cent,
dividend basis it will be necessary, at the present rate of
profit-making, to write off all the ordinary and two-thirds
of the preference capital. These figures seem to show
that there isn’t much hope in being a Colonial ordinary
shareholder, and none too much in being a preference one.
* * * *
The report of the Royal Bank (Melbourne) for
the half-year ended 30th Sept, shows no improvement
anywhere. The profits are £2043, whereas they were
£2099 for last half-year, and £2038, £2136 and £2875 for
the three previous ones. Therefore things are distinctly
not getting better; in fact, they are, on the whole,
rather worse, and the general tendency has been down-
ward for 2) years. The profit is something like £1 7s. 3d.
per cent, per annum on capital, which is a very humble
percentage, and again no dividend is paid. During the
half-year deposits have increased by £BOOO, and the busi-
ness in general seems well maintained. Also the dismal
smallness of the profits is not owing to the Royal having
large sums locked up in magnificent premises and gaudy
furniture ; it rents its office, and furniture, fittings,
stationery, &c., are all set down at £212. Therefore, the
natural assumption is that the worst trouble lies in the
unprofitableness of some of the advances. For at least five
successive balance-sheets the Royal’s directors have re-
marked that when the new Companies Act, authorising
the writing-off of vanished capital, passes the Victorian
Parliament, it will be necessary to write off capital
to provide for losses, and this time the same old
observation comes along once more. Yet in all the
time that this weary statement has been appearing, no
information has been offered as to the amount of
the losses, or the amount of capital to be written off, nor
has any attempt been made to put things straight in the
meantime by debiting profit and loss with the shortage,
and even the simple idea of writing off the £21,521 now
at credit of profit and loss, by way of putting things
partly straight, has seemingly not occurred to anybody.
And the patient shareholders haven’t kicked once at this
amazing reticence.
On the face of things the situation doesn’t, look
promising. If the Royal is going to pay a humble 3 per
cent, dividend, then on the present basis it has to write
off £165,000 out of its £300,000 paid-up capital ; and to
become even a 2 per cent. div.-paying concern it must
write off £lOO,OOO. In fact, if there is to be anything
worth calling a dividend the writing-off must be very
heavy. And as the reality of things( so far as the share-
holders are concerned) can hardly be much worse than
the outward appearance the board might as well explain
the. situation frankly.

Chief Inspector Stewart, of the Australian Joint
Stock Bank, accompanies Chairman W. A. Long on ms
visit to England on behalf of the bank. #
The Reid Govt, is offering two N.S.W. loans
for subscription in Sydney. One is for L 420 320 th e other
for £2,196,892; so the total offered is £2,617,212._ lney
both carry 3 per cent, interest, and the public is invited
to take them at par. This is a tolerably unkind way of
giving the local investor a show. When N.S.W. floated
its other 3 per cent, loan John Bull was allowed to get it
at a rate which netted the Govt, about £95 2s. for each
£lOO debenture, but the local man isn’t to be allowed to
invest unless he pays the full £lOO. In other words, the
N.S.W. Govt, gives J. Bull £3 4s. per cent, interest net, but
if the Cornstalk is willing to lend the money at £3 per
cent, less income-tax he may come in—not otherwise. If
he doesn’t take the offer it will, presumably, be decided
that floating loans locally is a failure. So far, things
have gone well, £150,000 being subscribed in three days,
so there is evidently going to be no absolute failure about
the business. But if it succeeds it will be a dismal com-
mentary on the policy by which the last loan was put on
the London market at a serious loss, after the Reid Govt,
had distinctly announced its resolve to rely on local re-
sources.
• * * *
According to Coghlan’s “ Seven Colonies,” New
South Wales spent, during last financial year, £559,000
of borrowed money on works which yield no re-
venue; Queensland spent £310,000 ; Westralia, £240,000;
S.A., £181,000; Maoriland, £170,000 ; Tasmania, £llO,OOO ;
and Victoria absolutely nothing. The safe principle to go
upon is that public works which yield no revenue should
be paid for out of revenue only, and it is noteworthy that
every province which had an alleged surplus last yea,r got
it by the old, commonplace swindle of charging ordinary
expenditure to loans. On a fair basis the Seven Pro-
vinces had seven large deficits, only six of them cooked
their accounts with a view to concealing the fact, and the
Victorian balance-sheet was the one honest document of
the crowd.
* * * »
The N.S.W. Land-tax assessments were issued
last week, and a howl of rage such as the province hasn’t
heard for many years has gone up to the heavens. Ac-
cording to general report the assessment is a truly won-
derful affair. Cases are quoted in which the Government
valuation for land only is four times or 3) times the
municipal assessment for land and improvements, where
a property yielding £5O a year is valued at £5OOO, where
land that fetched £lO a foot in the boom days and is now
unsaleable at £8 is valued at £27 10s.: and the like. One
daily paper tells a tale of a man who is vainly trying to
sell land with buildings thereon for £l9OO and who finds
the land alone taxed at £3OOO ; and another concerning
an individual who paid £9OOO in the boom days for land,
with buildings on it that then let for £4OO a year, and
who finds that after five or six years’ depreciation, the
land alone is valued by Government at £9OO0 —just what
it and the buildings were worth before the slump. Three
things that make the tax-payer particularly rampant are :
(1) That even if his assessment is hopelessly wrong and
the fact is admitted, he must pay up in full and skirmish
at the Treasury for a refund afterwards. (2) Even if he
appeals successfully and it is decided that the valuation
is absurd, he must pay his own costs. (3) If he pays this
time he has no chance of appealing again for five years.
These three facts make a good many people use language
about the Tax Department which is wholly unprintable.
* * * *
The Queensland Deposit Bank, which has
already done two reconstructions, looks as if a third
one is imminent. The present idea is that depositors
should take part payment in land and that the bank
should owe them the rest at 3 per cent, instead of 4J4 as
at present. Think of getting back half your hard-earned
savings in the shape of an unsaleable, squashy allotment
below flood-mark way down the Brisbane River, and the
guarantee of a three-times-reconstructed bank that the
rest will be forthcoming in cash when the institution feels
quite rested !
» * * *
The large and much-reconstructed St. Joseph’s
Investment and Building Society (Sydney) proposes still
another re-arrangement. The idea this time is that pay-
ment of the instalments of principal shall cease till
things mend, and the fact that the last £lB,OOO paid to
depositors was raised on mortgage shows that the case is
tolerably urgent. This new collapse is set down to the
continued fall in the value of property, which has slumped
in the last 12 months beyond all precedent, and to the
fact that houses are a drug in the market and St. Joseph
is generally unsaleable.

* * * *
J.N. : After reading your papers, your case
against the Colonial Mutual doesn’t amount to anything.
A State Life Insurance Department would have to treat
its members in exactly the same fashion if it wished to
remain solvent. The figures pan out as follows: You
paid about £BO in premiums and then borrowed £4O. You
never paid interest, and later on interest arrears and
further borrowings made the loan £77 against premiums
paid of £163. Then you kept on not paying interest, and
assumed that the amount (about £6 ss. a year, steadily-
increasing as the debt accumulated) would be covered by
the bonuses. The papers you send show that the bonus
averaged £5 a year (£5 payable at death, that is), and the
table on the back shows that £5 payable at death, per-
haps 25 years ahead, was equal to £2 in immediate
cash. You chose to take it for granted that £2 would
pay £6 ss. or more, despite clear intimation to the con-
trary ; but naturally it didn’t. If you expected, when the
second loan was fixed up, that the society could give you
back over £6 a year out of £l7, you wanted more than
the richest insurance co. on earth could give. In the end
you owed £153 10s. for principal and interest, and that
swallowed up the surrender value of your policy', on which
you paid £314. In exchange for the difference you were
insured for £5OO during 18.) years, so you didn’t lose it —
you got value in return. The whole trouble seems to be
in your inability to understand some very simple figures.

New South Wales is now, and has been for a
long time back, the boss mineral-producing province of
Australasia. For 1895, its total production of minerals
was worth ±4,413,000. Victoria came next with
±3 090 000 : then Queensland, ±2,495,000; Maoriland,
±1'587',000 ; and Westralia, ±902,000. Tasmania and S. A.
brought upjthe rear. „ „
For the four weeks to Oct. 15, the B.H. Pro-
prietary output was—Gold, 4090 z. ; silver, 646,0860 z.; soft
lead, 1500 tons; antimonial lead, 50 tons; copper (in
matte), 44 tons ; and an additional 27,9360 z. silver in
matte.’ Some of the figures are estimated, of course ; for
this company reckons it more important to make money
than to permit any break in the operations to count it.
Approximately the value of the four weeks’ out-turn is
±108,250. It takes ±48,000 to pay a monthly shilling div.
North Central shaft has now crept down to
515 ft. Bottom still in terribly hard quartz, through
which from 4ft. to 6ft. per week can be laboriously carved.
Surely this is the kind of work which the diamond drill
ought to do.
The B.H. North finances have been in a poor
way, but the difficulties have been tided over temporarily.
Junction North has made further contracts for
the sale of high-class ore—the latest being for export.
The demand for this mine’s stuff is unusually keen.
The British B.H. now treats in its concentrating
mill about half sulphides and half concentrates. With
the increasing development the risk of “ runs ” or
“ creeps ”in this mine grows. The management had one
little warning, the other day. But even if one of the
main shafts, is from this or any other cause, temporarily
disabled the haulage appliances here are so complete that
the other shaft will be able to keep the mill fully
supplied.
It is proposed in Melb. that the several concen-
trating plants at Broken Hill should be tested by an inter-
change of ore. The idea is that parcels of, say, 500 tons
of ore, of equal value and of the same character,
should be sent to the different mills. The South mill
will probably have a run on Block 10 ore, and Block 10
will also suggest an exchange with the British. The
North and the South being in the same “ stable,” no diffi-
culty is experienced in effecting an exchange so far as
they are concerned. This step is taken on account of the
impossibility (previously referred to) of gauging the real
value of the work done by the figures issued periodically.
Take Block 10 ore, for instance. It is impossible to say
how much the comparative lack of success is due—or, to
put it in another way, how many of the difficulties that
have arisen are due—to the undoubtedly very complex
ores, and how much to the undoubtedly very complex
machinery which Capt. Warren designed, and which is
now turning his hair grey. Only by running- them on the ;
same ore can the respective merits of the same mills be
gauged and the fads and fancies of the several engineers
be satisfactorily tested. At the same time, it will have to
be borne in niind that the plant which may the most
successfully deal with one class of ore may be a compara-
tive failure when run upon another class. Thus, a mine
whose sulphides are hard requires a larger crushing power
in proportion to jigs, &e., than such a mine as the British,
most of whose ore is, so far, fairly soft.
Talking of the hardness and softness of the
sulphides, the variations are phenomenal. In the
Junction’s rich stuff in places, one can drive a crowbar a
foot or two. At Block 14, however, a couple of hundred
yards or so away, some of the sulphides are only slightly
softer than the best drill steel. A 2ft. 6in. hole recently
took two men over six hours to drill ; and it was not un-
usual for three or four blows of the hammer to render the
drill useless until re-sharpened. Nor are these Block 14
records due to poor mining. On the contrary, Block 14,
having always been one of the best-managed mines on the
field, naturally always has had the best of the miners.

From Drake, N.S.W., comes the report that a
vein two feet thick of almost solid silver has been’ struck
in the White Rock mine, and from Wollongong comes the
news that the mine is now owned by the Smelting Co. of
Australia (whose “ stack at Lake Illawarra is 165 ft. high,
is finished, and a higher one still is in course of erection ”).
Well, the White Rock having up to date been a gigantic
disappointment, it is about time it had something by way
of a surprise, and two feet of solid silver would about do
the trick. If a discount of 50 per cent, is made “soas to
be well within the mark,” the ore would be worth con-
siderably over ±2OOO a ton and prove the mine not very
low grade, after all.

The good old grand-motherly S.M. Herald has
been requested to state that the Young Bushman’s gold-
mine, whose prospectus is out, has produced over 800 oz.
of gold, “ the yields averaging from 15dwts. to 3 and 4oz.
to the ton.” When? Which? What? Last year or 20
years ago ? The writer knows a mine that has produced
over 8000 oz. of gold, and it can be had for pegging out.
Forming cos. to reopen old mines, instead of small work-
ing syndicates to prospeot them, is the curse of mining
in N.S.W.
• * • »
Adelong, N.S.W. : “ The monthly escort left
to-day, taking 3530 z., nearly the whole of which was
alluvial.” Once it was the chief gold-camp of N.S.W. Its
total output will run any other field in the province pretty
close. Veins, as a rule, very small and, worse still, in
hard granite country. Adelong thinks it is on the eve of
a great and glorious resurrection. The English co. work-
ing the Gibraltar mine has almost completed a 30-head
stamp mill, cyanide and chlorination works, and is other-
wise preparing to do big things early next year. It owns
several mines beside the Gibraltar, including Shepherd’s
well-known alluvial mine—probably pretty well worked
out, but purchased for the water-power brought on to it
many years ago. The race has been enlarged, and the
power will be used to drive the whole of the reducing
machinerj'. Whether it will do so at all times of the year
is a matter on which opinions differ. Anyhow, the mine
is not quite as well watered as the stock.

This is tin sort of thing weighing down W.A.
mining The Scottish Westralia Ltd. offered in London
for public issue the Lindsay’s Find Proprietary of 173
acres containing FIFTEEN gold-bearing reefs for £200,000.
The Scottish Westralia Co. yanks out of this £20,000 cash,
±80,600 shares, and £50,000 in cash or shares as it
chooses. All the reefs were tested by mortar and dish,
and 3,5, 8, and 20oz. per ton coruscate all over the re-
port like spangles on Sadie McDonald's legs. Mr.
Edmund Mitchell, once on Pastoralists’ Review and
Melb. Age, the gent who wrote “A Temple of Death,"
is the guiding spirit of the Scottish Westralia board.
This leads one to think “ The Lindsay’s Find Pro-
prietary” prospectus is perhaps meant as a sequel
to the “Temple of Death” story. David Lindsay,
F.R.G.S., was apparently Mitchell’s collaborateur in writ-
ing the prospectus. Is that the Lindsay who reported on
the Mount Burgess, St. George, Lindsay’s Gold Mines, and
Lindsay’s Extended?
» * * *
Artesian water has been struck in Westralia by
means of the Calyx drill, the invention of Harley Davis,
for some years the “ ringer ” of many a Bananaland shed.
The drill is doing great work in South Africa, where it got
a big, and lucky, introduction by striking a 22ft. seam of
coal in the first bore. It is also doing splendid boring in
N.S. W. and Vic., and the hole on the Perth station, W.A.,
is now giving 384,000 gallons of fresh water daily. Luck
as well as good work has followed the Calyx.

known as the Lake View Consols, is one of the finest gold-

place on this camp since you were here.”

formation has taken place in the history of gold-mining.

Bayley, finder of the mine which gave Westralian
history its turn, turned up at last meeting of Bayley’s
Reward Co., and told the directors that they knew nothing
about mining. Do any of these gentlemen know anything
about, the share-market? He also mentioned that he still
held 19,500 shares. That rather proves that Bayley him-
self knows little of mining or marketing, or he would have
sold, say at 30s. a share. He also offered to bet a modest
£50,000 that all, or most of, the gold which had been
credited to Bayley’s South mine had come out of the
Reward claim. ’ No one responded to the bet, and the
occurrence seems to have been barely mentioned by the
newspapers.

New Golden Gate, Tas., latest crushing, 889 oz.
from 1545 tons. New reef lately struck, and shares have
climbed up a bit. More envy than interest is evinced in
the improved condition of the mine, as it is mostly a one-
man affair. That the plucky one man deserves his luck
is, of course, admitted—in public.
* * * *
Waihi mine (M.L.) four weeks’ run, 3060 tons
for bullion valued at £12,451. A great low-grade concern
for many years because of the gold left in tailings, now
saved by cyanide, the boss metallurgical invention of the
century.

A Kalgoorlie mine-manager has a cat whose
favorite article of diet is salt. Pity it’s a tame cat.

Reid’s minute.

CAMEO
There is a good deal of sympathy but not
much logic in the Tamworth petition lately pre-
sented to Gov. Hampden on behalf of the Dederers,
now under sentence of death for murder. Its
most conspicuous plea is that both brothers are
only 19, that one of them has never been to school
or church, and that they were born at Boggabri
and have never been more than ten miles away
from it. The common theory is that the person
who is born in rural innocence and never goes out
into the great, wicked, soulless world thereby
escapes a knowledge of evil, and either this as-
sumption has to go overboard or else the fact that
a criminal has lived all his life at Boggabri must
be abandoned as an extenuating circumstance. It
is a melancholy fact, by the way, that N.S.W. is
growing a dismal race of oldest bush inhabitants
who vegetate all their days. Even when they
take to the roving life of a prospector they don’t
rove except feebly and in a narrow circle. There
are townships within 200 miles of Sydney which
contain hardly a soul who has been away more
than 40 miles in half as many years. Apparently
the wandering instinct is dying out of the Corn-
stalk, if he ever possessed it* and he fossilises

among the gum-trees just as his old British bucolic
ancestor fossilised among the oaks. The future of
the two races seems to depend a good deal on the
question whether the oak or the gum-tree is the
better company for a fossil.
* * * *
Inscrutable is the action of the Deity in
wrecking the missionary steamer Dayspring, fitted
out at great cost to convey “ the glad tidings of
the Gospel ” to the pore heathen. Had the ship
carried a band of Sydney spielers, bent on initiat-
ing the gentle nig. into the mysteries of “two-
up,” or how to make fly on Melb. Cup their sharks-
teeth, or other meejum of exchange, humans could
understand it, and would not need an unctuous
cleric to explain that it was a judgment from
above. Anyhow, The Bulletin will never take
shares in a good-tidings company.
* * * *
King O’Malley’s proposal to abolish bar-
maids after 1899 has been carried in S.A. Assem-
bly, much to the delight of the Cold Tea Party.
O’Malley, by the way, has quite supplanted Cald-
well as the windiest person in S.A. Legislature.
Also, the O’Malley, though ostensibly a Govt,
supporter, brings a gay bouquet for the leader
of the S.A. Opposition (Copley) every day the
Assembly sits.
* * * *
The advocates in S.A. Assembly of the
abolition of barmaids shrieked that such was
necessary in the interests of Morality. The social
impurity crank is nothing if not illogical. Why
not protect the frail chambermaid from the bald-
headed lodger, and the frail cook from the vicious
leer of the worldly butcher’s man ? And experi-
ence proclaims with a loud voice that society sadly
needs protection from the dirty saints !
* * * *
Re grog in “temperance” hotels. A
retired Melb. grocer affirms that he for a long
period regularly supplied to the residents of a local
coffee-palace at least £lO worth of alcohol weekly.
A year or two back, also, the proprietors of two
hotels near the same coffee-palace had a number of
the palace waiters in their pay to bring them
boarders’ custom. That coffee-palace’s grog-custom
absolutely paid for the rebuilding of one of the
hotels in question.
A certain silver-haired pastor, finding a
“ drunk ” blocking up one of the corridors in the
city coffee-palace where he was staying, remarked :
“ Man, why can’t you go out without getting in
this terrible condition?” “ Who’sh been out!”
replied the drunk —“ easy shee you not lived much
in coffee-palashes—hie !”
• * * *
The pending matrimonial case, which has
interested one section of South-Western society
for some months, involves a prominent ex-politician
at one time in the beer-trade, and another ex-
M.P. and money-lender. One fascinating lady
concerned is also known in the beer trade, and
figured a few years ago in a case which scared
several prominent citizens, not so much because of
what came out as of what didn’t.
* * * *
Tuberculosis is found in 30 to 70 per cent,
of the cows in average dairy herds. Wherefore the
British Sanitary Congress at its meeting, just over,
recommended that sanitary authorities advise
public establishments only to buy milk from herds
found pure by Kooh’s tuberculin test, that the
authorities advertise lists of such herds, that
government test free from time to time the herds of
farmers who guarantee to kill off cows found m
fected, that such infected beasts be officially
branded at time of testing, and slaughtered under
special supervision, that killing of all animals for
human consumption be done in supervised official
abattoirs, and that butchers be compensated for car-
cases of beasts condemned after slaughtering which
had not shown signs of wasting diseases when
alive.
* * * *
The director of Stawell (Vic.) Technical
College has been examining 30 samples of Mel-
bourne meat with the result that he has found five
tuberculous (i.e. containing the germs of con-
sumption). The conveyance of meat thro ugh the
streets exposed to dust and air polluted by the
dried sputum of phthisical persons, &c., is held
responsible for the Walpurgis-night of the microbe
on the outer skin of one sample, tersely described
by this observer as “ a hot-bed of bacteria.” Of 20
samples of milk three were tuberculous, and one of
them contained a brand-new bacillus.
* * * *
Sargood has embellished the Vic. Factories
and Shops Act with a new clause prohibiting the
delivery of butchers’ meat on Sunday. This re-
form is bound to be much appreciated in Smell-
bourne during the summer. The morning air of a
hot Sabbath that succeeds a sultry Saturday will
no longer be heavy with the odors of hot tainted
joints. The meat will have duly crawled quite
beyond the cook’s jurisdiction.

Shop assistants in a West Queensland town
wanted a half-holiday weekly. The first pre-
liminary was the sounding of the Chow store-
keepers on the matter, and when they had con-
sented the European business men followed as a
matter of course. To this has it come—Jack
Smith and Billy Brown have to petition Ho-Ki
and Sin On for leave to breathe a little fresh air
now and again.

Chinese statistics of a Centralian town
Six Chow storekeepers, four pig-tailed bakers, and
gardeners and fruiterers innumerable. Just a
little outside is a splendid garden run on co-opera-
tive principles by Europeans, but the. white
women prefer John’s vegetables, with their lurk-
ing leprosy, so the Caucasian growers are mostly
left out in the cold. Talk about Cairns ! Some
of the Western and North-Western towns beat it
hollow.

* * * * .
The khakee uniform of Vic. militia is
objected to by the officers, because it is not suffi-
ciently gaudy to attract recruits ; by the recruit*
because it does not attract the girls; and by the
girls because it does not attract the jealousy of the
other girls who have not got dirt-colored admirers.
As Commandant Holled-Smith is not likely to
recommend any change, the Vic. fire-eaters will
have to tramp along as heretofore, looking like
animated mustard-plasters, in the rear of a corpu-
lent man mounted on a livery-stable charger, and
with a rooster’s tail in his hat.

By his lean, dejected figger
You may know the Northern nigger
Is a-weeping and a-wailing and a-beating Sorrow’s
drum ;
By his look of desolation
And the way he says “ Damnation ! ”
sTou can bet his soul is burdened and the Bishop
hasn’t come.
By his waist that waxes bigger
You may know the Northern nigger
Is a-calming of his spirit with religion and with
rum;
By his pot that’s always boiling,
By his jaws that keep a-toiling,
You can bet there’s balm in Gilead and the
Bishop’s really come.

The oldest German settlement in S.A. is
Tanunda, which celebrated its jubilee this month.
Many of the residents cannot speak a word of
English.
* * * *
“ Motor-cars are finding their way about
London just now at the heels of a man and a red-
flag,” writes a travelling Australian to The Bul-
letin. “The Act abolishing the standard-bearer
takes effect in Nov., when it is expected the
motors will be running into and over one another
in a mechanical matter-of-fact manner without the
exciting relief of plunging steeds and the clatter-
ing of slithering shoes. The assistance a pair of
horses are in sharply arresting the way on a heavy
vehicle will be adequately recognised only when
the slippery asphaltum brakes fail to prevent a car-
motor from tunnelling the Lord-Mayor’s circus-
van and holding a lengthy sitting on the alder-
manic carotid.”
* * * *
Small wonder that Gov. Brassey is always
ready to speak on all sorts of subjects. His
oratorical stock in trade consists entirely of plati-
tudes—and most of those not even mediaeval but
mummy-old. For instance, speaking on Imperial
Federation —“The essential and practical work to
be done at present is to cultivate in every part of
the empire amongst the English-speaking peoples
a sentiment of mutual love and attachment.”
There now ! But this is exactly what Christianity
has been trying to do on a large scale for the last
nineteen centuries. And steel-bristling, snarling
Europe shows with what success! _ Does Gov.
Brassey—who never loses an opportunity of telling
his vice-subjects how good a Christian he is—
imagine that that bloodless phantom called Im-
perial Federation will succeed where Christianity
has failed ? If so, an earnest evangelist should
be sent to Vic. Govt. House without delav.

This is the fashion of corporal punishment
on the Mulligan, Q. A gin levants from her
white boss with a black admirer. Boss with his
black satellite pursues the dusky pair, overtakes
them, trices the gin up to a sapling, and belts her
furiously with a hobble chain fastened to a long
stick.
* * * *
N.S.W. Justice Stephen reckons that hasty
marriages are responsible for half the divorces and
nearly all the troubles which disturb the happi-
ness of young married people. On the other hand,
long “ engagements ” are calamitous to girls who
get jilted at the end of them, except in the few
instances where they lead to a breach of promise
action and substantial damages. Of the two evils,
a long engagement is more agin’ nature than a
hasty marriage, anyhow, and the ill-considered
baby born in rash wedlock is rather more moral
than the infant fruit of a long-drawn-out seduc-
tion.
An Anglo-Australian concerning the Lon-
don barmaid: —
Yes, dear Bulletin, there are fully 80,000 bar-women in
city and suburban London, but few of these many thou-
sands are likely to be “tempted.” “My face is my for-
tune,” the barmaid said, and the young man recognised
that she was in a most insolvent state. The great number
of the London bar-women cannot compare with their
Australian sisters, but are granite-faced citadels of uncer-
tain youth, though undoubted age. Their hard, stony
impregnableness repels the most ruthless invader, or
else shows such ravages of previous vandals as to clearly
indicate the fruit has been long demolished and but the
shrivelled skin remains. They are mostly the warped
wrecks of trim craft that for a brief period cruised in
summer seas, luxuriously fitted and richly manned, to
awaken near the arctic circle in the turmoil of rough
weather, deserted by their men, and only too glad to lay
their worm-eaten, shaky timbers in some safe harbor, secure
from a fatal foundering. This paragraph is slightly in-
volved, but it’s nothing to the man who gets in tow with
a London bar—maid.
A certain boomer of fickle
affections lately secured No. 1 of a
prospective harem without arrang-
ing to pension or otherwise appease
his lawful mate. The lady, being
naturally aggrieved, vowed to get
even. This she decided to do by
opening a boarding-house in a popu-
lous centre, the feature of which
was to be the largest sign which
could be conveniently erected, bear-
ing the following legend: “Mrs.
B. Brown-Jones-Robinson, board-
inghouse-keeper.” Hubby bought
out the prospective hash-house, they
say, at <£soo and a pension.
Busk : “ Saw a droll thing last night—a
ball in a butcher’s shop. Being Friday evening,
after closure, the steel rods around were thickly
hung with mutton carcasses. Nevertheless, on
the nicely-sanded floor there swish-wished the
blue-shirted men, with shiny, plastered-down,
suety hair, embracing ladies in the mazes of the
giddy waltz, to the fascinating strains of the
"Alabama Coon ’on a wheezy concertina. Now
and again, fair shoulders, skirts, and flowery hats
inevitably whisked against the mutton, which
waggled sympathetically.”

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice mil be taken of communioations insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

horses, you have to pay damages in any case H.F. :

A widely-known man died at Manly last
week in the person of the smiling and kindly
Ered. P. Ruffley, the interment of whose remains
was witnessed by many friends. Mr. Ruffley,
who for years had represented in Sydney some
British and Melb. softgoods interests, was
formerly well-known in Victoria in connection
with the then ruling firm of L. Stevenson and
Sons.
Thus cogently Melb. Age on Premier
Reid s refusal to tell the Sydney Syrian deputa-
tion whether it was intended to exclude Syrians
under the Colored Races Exclusion Bill
It has often been urged by the opponents of representa-
tive government that Actsof Parliament were passed mostly
for giving the lawyers something to do, but this is the
first occasion that a Premier has openly announced, his
intention of passing an ambiguous measure for that pur-
pose. *
The latest version of the alleged Rose-
brook (Vic.) murder-case is a cold-blooded horror
such as the Yankee editor invents when things
are quiet down Kansas way. According to the
story of a married daughter of the deceased, which
tale is backed up by a grand-daughter, they one
night heard shrieks and groans in the next room,
which seemed to suggest that “mother” was
being killed. They let the matter rest at that,
however, and asked no questions. Next day
mother” had disappeared, and the old man said
she had gone away ; but looking casually through
a window they thought they saw mother, de-
ceased, lying on the bed. Nothing was said on
this occasion either. Next night they thought
they heard mother being dragged out of the
house, but no special enquiry was made.
Another time, chancing to look into a shed,
they found mother there, somewhat decom-
posed and with a bag over her—in fact, they seem
to have kept dropping unexpectedly on the lady
for quite a while. This time also the subject was
dropped. Three days later they casually looked
round for her again, but she was gone, and she
didn’t transpire any more till she was found in a
sack—poor mother had “got the sack,” with a
vengeance—in the river with a rope round her
neck. This final uprising seems to have worried
them, and after some hesitation they told the
above story, which, as a plain, business-like nar-
ration of what happened to mother, has some
interest.
Time deals gently with the woman who
uses Babeskin soap. It softly removes the
tell-tale lines of the years, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. S. Harris, 78 King-street, Sydney.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
T , ~ Sydney, Oct. 27, 1896.
My Deab Myee—
An unvarying law of sassiety is that, while you
cannot be genteel as plain Smith or Brown, you
can be as genteel as possible if you only wed your
plebeian monosyllable to some high-sounding
name. The various branches of hyphenated
Smiths and Johnsons flourish exceedingly in social
columns in the lists of the Nicest. What makes
the general public so tired is reading those same
lists of names day after day, week after week,
year after year. If there could be a general
muster and roll-call once a year or once a quarter,
or even once a month, “ constant readers ” might
be expected to be intelligent enough to take the
statement as authenticated that “all the Nicest
were present, also the first half-dozen names of
the next-to-nicest.” Or, from time to time, what’s
the matter with having a revision, and publishing
“The following names have been added to the
Nicest set ? Or, if that list is too precious
as a tone-giver to be abbreviated, it might be
kept standing and save the expense of having
it set up over and over again. But names have
their power. An aspiring hostess lately found
that it added greatly to her social standing when
an account of a festival given by her was published
along with the names of all the folk she had in-
vited, whether they turned up or not. She
tactfully supplied the copy, names and all, to
the papers. The affair was such a crush that
nobody knew who was there and who wasn’t. But
to the next shivoo she gave, everybody came. The
clever woman went in upon her chances and won
cind now has floated gracefully into the desired
haven.
In Sight of St. Paul s,” where there is no fog
—when there is you have to run your nose against
the venerable structure before you find out it is
there a great many wonderful things occur.
Some of them are happening now every evening at
Sydney Royal, but, fortunately, I haven’t to un-
ravel deep mysteries of the melodrama, but only
to point out in a humble way the triumphs of Miss
I weedie in Act 3, also the artistic and attractive
setting of the gilded infamy pourtrayed. There
is an Oriental boudoir with Moorish archway and
curtains and luxurious divans and lounges most
beautiful to behold ; the apartment of a weird en-
chantress leading dupes to their ruin, oalled for
short “The Panther”—in reality Miss Watson
with an extra-special gown or two. There are
some well-painted cloths of London’s dome, also
bt. Paul s churchyard, strangely deserted, with not
a bus nor a bobby visible, where an intelligent
hammy Brass strolls off to the Old Bailey via
Cheapside. All the characters finally meet in a
realistic interior in the Cathedral, an irrelevant
duchess weeps over an unnamed hero’s tomb and
the villain suicides behind the sepulchre of Chinese
Cordon.
There is one very strong scene in the play—the
supper-party, where the Panther and her friends,
a.I birds of a feather, wind up their feminine
riu^ er . { a pistol-duel and a conflagration,
the girls dresses are all in tones of cream and
yellow, the hangings are heliotrope, and on the
table are green glasses and yellow candle-shades—
quite a refreshing color-scheme, against which the
injured heroine (Miss Ross) stands out in deep
black. Elizabeth Watson’s tram of maize-watered
silk takes up most of the stage, and V. Vivienne
nyals her m spangly satin, her pretty hair caught
with a diamond comb ; a specially-attractive re-
veller is in cream brocade and real-looking golden
tresses. Another costume assumed by the Panther
lor the destruction of surrounding mankind is
white with exaggerated train, low neck and
poppies-and-lace sleeves. The men’s clothes are
unremarkable. Bland Holt is dressed' in Gentle-
man Joe guise ; Detective Clarkson, with nothing
much to do m the piece, but an unfulfilled mission
in trying to detect the plot, is the swaggerest
. stage “d.” on record.

on their own vehicles ranked up alongside the
Hampden trademark brought out the carriages of
m force on Saturday night last to the
INat Goodwin send-off at the Lyceum. An en-
thusiastic house was so reluctant to part with
the Yankee star that it insisted on a farewell
speech—although during his entire season here the
large crowd of his admirers have been principally
outside the theatre, and, sad to tell, were not
lound inside hanging on his words to any over-
whelming extent. The customary “presenta-
tion worked backward on this occasion, too, and
was made (the day before) to manager Goodman,
but Goodwin has command of plenty money and
can afford to do these little things. His present
wife is a millionairess, and his sound financial
backing has made him philosophical about bad
biz. Why the magnificent Maxine has not been
a greater draw is one of the problems which I, as
a mere woman, am unable to solve. Sydney men
perhaps prefer brains to beauty, as, socially, Ger-
trude has been far more popular than her
statuesque sister, whose manner is refrigerating
while Gertrude is bright. Which view should be
consoling to girls who are ticketed with the “ Not
pretty but so clever ” label. If you look very hard
at Maxine s full-face photo, you can easily con-

Miss Muntz, a Melb. artist just returned from a
course of study in Paris, has achieved some dis-
tinction there, having been hung at one of this
year s batons. Portrait-painting is her rdle, and
her best-known sitter is the deposed Agent-General!
Lillies, ( who charmed dreary hours in the studio'
by his witty and clever ” conversation with tho
artist. Times are changed since the days of Dr.
J ohnson, who said that portrait-painting was an
indelicate profession for a woman, because it com-
pelled her to stare hard into a man’s face for
hours together.
, , vice-regal nose being out of joint,
the Moral Fete on Saturday at the Agricultural
Grounds was ungraced by His Ex.’s presence,
wny do Guvmentouse inmates always damage
their noses when they trip ? Lady Hopetoun once
wore hers bandaged for weeks after a fall in the
hunting-field, and Captain Wallington’s aquiline
profile was adorned with sticking-plaster after his
bicycle ran into a tram dummy. Lady Hampden
attended the fete accompanied by two daughters,
one with her hat at the most rakish angle ; and the
party, with assorted magnates, sat in a mia-mia
and surveyed the sports, which ran much ,to
childish gambollings on the lawn brightened by
variegated art-muslin and too-familiar crinkled
tissue-paper. With that airy fabric the modern
church-festival clothes itself as with a garment.
“ e n-'te itself was spread out rather thin over the
grounds; there was not enough revel to the
square-inch to be quite perceptible.
.-The rather wilted procession of vehicles trimmed
with flowers and greens looked so thirsty that it
was a wonder nobody was moved to shout drinks
all round with a garden-squirt. Quite the pret-
tiest show was made by the Star cart, the adorners
had forethought of the fading power of greenery
an d used nothing but closely-ranked white and
f e n “looms, Most amateur trimmings were too
full of bracken ferns and other fast-withering
vegetables. The flannel-flowered buggy became
droopy ; a milk-cart looked trim, its taps decked
with ribbons ; an authentic coster-cart with its
neddy was led by its owner in “ pearlies ” and all
complete. Among the bicyclists wheeling around
competitively a smart little jockey of five or so
took everybody’s fancy. Her Ex. distributed
prizes and was presented with a whip adorned
with daffodils and lily-of-the-valley and streamers
of ribbon.
A select few trailed wearily to the Rose-show
opening ceremony at the Town Hall on Wednes-
day 21st. Lady H., her celebrated slimness set
well off by a gracefully-cut black gown, was accom-
panied by Her Ex. of Bananaland, whose muslin
frock did not give us any very thrilling sugges-
tions of Brisbane modes. Having watched the
distinguished visitors sniff critically at the roses,
there was nothing much to do except stand about
and wonder at the inartistic crudeness of the pink
calico decorations.
To a musical enthusiast Trebelli’s clear, bird-
like notes are as refreshing as beer to a bicyclist.
At her Saturday concert in Centennial Hall, a
large audience assembled to applaud Mademoiselle,
who was clad in flowing robes of silver brocade,
and wore no fringe. Many bunches of flowers
were handed up during the evening. The only
other performers were Mr. Weir, warbling ballads,
and Mr. Strauss, with his ’cello. An uncertainty
as to correct garb pervaded the front sitters, some
° f If™ arrived in full-dress, while others hastily
grabbed at their hats and put them under the
seats in order to appear en regie.
Boys’ Brigade, neatly attired in new clothes,
toed the line at the formal opening of its new
hall in Elizabeth-street on Thursday night, and
was lectured by Gov. Hampden in a pleasant,
straightforward speech. H.E.’s remark on the
a . e B’ e< i incivility of Democracy leads me to protest
mildly against the confounding of “manners”
with manner, which is one of the causes of the
disgusted contempt often shown for all outward
form of politeness by the truly polite man. The
hall-mark of the “ gentleman ” is his consideration
towards women, and • where are women better (or
as well) treated than in democratic America ? The
Englishman is really much more courteous to
woman than the Frenchman, because more natural
and less obtrusive. He does not force himself so
much on her notice and then bow and scrape
apologetically to her embarrassment. There are
times when the truest civility to a woman is shown
being politely unconscious of her presence.
Why should a man grab at his hat if there is a
lady m the lift ? A lift is a public conveyance—
he might just as well take it off in a tram, or to
every woman he passes in the street. The ideally-
well-mannered man is the man with no “ manner ”
at all.

The Trilby craze has not been cremated along
with her creator, worse luck. I see “Little
Billee,” as a boy’s front name, printed in a
juvenile ball-list. The name will probably stick,
fc^at y° ull & ster ’ s mature years, when
Tnlby” has become a synonym for vulgarity.
Girls pet names are often absurd misnomers also.
Counting up some of my own acquaintances,
“ Birdie ” belongs to a stout matron of some-and-
thirty ; “ Queenie ” is an insignificant chit of five-
feet-nothing ; “ Dot ” will be appropriate in a few
years for an aged maiden, now nearly 60; “ Muffle ”
is just the average girl, and “ Popsy ” a tall, fair,
dignified damsel, with “ Helen ” in every feature.
A married man I know is still maddened by his
female relatives addressing him as “ Kissie ” and
“Boy.”
Adelaide tradesmen complain that the Buxtons
spend even less than the Kintores did—truly a
terrible indictment. The sole festivities during
the recent stay of the warships were a very select
vice-regal ball and two semi-official dinners. A
kind of financial Scotch mist seems to have per-
manently enveloped Adelaide Govt. House.
I am told of an elegantly-dressed male who has
found out how to live in blissful indolence on no-
thing a week. He advertises for “ Board and
residence, widow preferred,” and, having got his
widow, he pays a deposit. When further pay-
ment is due he puts his arms round the lady’s neck
and gurgles : “ I love you.” If it does not come
off he is requested to leave, and there being no
man he escapes kicking. If it does come off,
there is a cuddlesome widow and free diggings for
three or four months. Most often it does come
off.
In the big scandal mysteriously hinted at in a
leading and select club, the spirits of Cissy,
Geordie, and Josephine are said to have been re-
vived for the occasion, and have been held
responsible for quite a lot of queer rumors.
Almost all the recently-imported American girls
may be expected to discourage their friends from
visiting these parts. They have not found the
local gilded youth at all up to what their fancy
painted. One of the earlier arrivals, with an idea
that she could track and snare squatters in George
or Collins-street, soon discovered her mistake and
took it out in brains by marrying a good-looking
professional. No. 2 left by a recent boat loudly
expressing her disgust at the stony-broke state of
our eligibles, who, moreover, somehow either re-
main adamant to trans-Pacific charms or try to
run a campaign economically. It worries a girl
when a man looks at her with cold, commemora-
tive eye, inwardly calculating the value of all the
presents he has promised her. The only American
visitor who has made a substantial conquest of
late years is the “champagne-cocktail” girl who
got nicely fixed up with a smart dog-cart and
titled adorer, youthful and a peer.
Writes a lady touring in Western Australia :
“ This tour has been awful. We came straight
through and played in Coolgardie to tremendous
business. We have to sleep three in a room, pay-
ing four guineas a week for the accommodation,
and sharing one jug of water and one wash-basin
among us. . . . As for the laundryman, he
charges 12s. 6d. per dozen, and 2s. each for shirts,
and as the red dust which covers the whole
country, and which you have to wade through
knee-deep, gets into everything you wear, you can
imagine the bill ! . . . We never tasted vege-
tables except potatoes ; there was no fresh milk,
and the meat was awful. . . . Nearly all the co.
have been ill. The men all got ‘ Barcoo, ’ a
malady which seems peculiar to out-of-the-way
places—incessant vomiting accompanied by dysen-
tery. . . . And yet, strange to say, as soon as you
become acclimatised to the fields, you don’t know
what it is to have a day’s illness, unless you hap-
pen to catch typhoid. The place is full of it. . . .
is very cute. He would not arrange to
pay our expenses in W.A., and, of course, it has
taken all our salary to pay our way. When you
pay £4 10s. a week board and your washing, you
can imagine there is very little left; consequently
there has been great discontent. . . .
has coined money on the fields, but his co. are
broke.
“The hotelkeepers (continues my correspon-
dent) have to pay their waitresses £3 10s. per
week, and treat them nicely or they clear out; and
God help the poor hotelkeeper ! he can’t get
another for love or money. They all marry well
after being on the fields for three months. All
our girls have each had half-a-dozen proposals of
marriage—even yours truly has had four. They
won’t believe you are married unless your hubby
is with you. After they have known you for five
minutes, they propose to you.”
Prom a letter written to a children’s paper by
the seven-year-old daughter of an Australian
Judge : —“My pa has three servants, three horses,
two dogs, one laughing jackass, three parrots, and
only one wife.”
Writes my Melb. correspondent:—
“The Melb. Dante Society is flourishing vigor-
ously. Its members number over a hundred, only
about a dozen of whom know anything of the
grim-visaged poet or the Italian tongue, but that
doesn’t prevent the society giving itself frequent
and charming little jamborees, consisting chiefly
of really good music and unique ice-creams. On
Saturday afternoon Consul Corte entertained
all the Melb. smart folk at a garden fete in com-
memoration of the wedding of Italy’s Crown
Prince. The Consul, a bachelor, lives at a fine St.
Kilda house, built in a pre-boom era, and standing
in well-kept grounds. Most of the guests were
‘coming on from’ or ‘going on to’ either the
white knight’s garden-party or the Brassey cycle
sports—and the affair had the appearance of a
dress rehearsal for Cup day. A little comedy, all
about a youth who wanted to be a monk, but who
fell in love with a girl instead, was played in the
wide verandah by Sigs. Corte and Fiaschi and Miss
Law, who seems to be the only native-born Melb.
girl who can ‘ crack Itawlian ’ (as the Scotch say)
with fluency.
“Miss Dudgeon, one of Melb.’s nicest girls,
was very quietly married last week to Lieut.
Manser, an Indian military officer. The couple
will reside at Mandalay, British Burmah. The
mother of the bride, who looks slightly younger
than her daughter, and who is in reality only a few
years her senior, was married less than a year ago
to Hon. Agar Wynne, whose second wife she is.
Both the wives of this fortunate man have brought
him large fortunes, and a short time ago the ex-
P.M.G. made a great haul over a successfully-
floated Westralian mine. He leaves shortly for
London on further mine-launching business.’’
‘ ‘ Rumor couples the names of Lord Shaftesbury
and the. daughter of our only baronet. The
damsel in question, a graceful girl enough, in-
herits little of her mother’s vivacity and social
energy. As for pa, he has always protested that
he did not wish his children to hook English
titles, and, voluntarily or not, the elder ones have
met his wishes in this particular. In many
respects, no doubt, a title is a worry. The ques-
tion of heir-supply, for instance, causes more
bother than enough, and a quiverful of girls with
never a brother among them is even more disas-
trous in baronial halls than elsewhere.
“ No further off than Toorak, there was tearing
of hair a while back when the six-year-old
daughter of a future baronet was introduced to a
superfluous ‘ sissy,’ in place of the confidently-
expected ‘ ikkle brudder.’ And the lamentations
weren’t confined to the nursery, for, apart from
the disappointment proper, an over-sanguine
maternal grandmother had been trotting round
the neighborhood for weeks predicting, or rather
announcing as a dead certainty, the advent of an
heir. When the worst was known, kind friends
shut their eyes to the press announcement, and all
prefaced their congratulatory letters with the
remark that they were so delighted to hear from
Mrs. Grand’mere of the birth of a son.

“Lord Magheramorne, who married A.D.C.
Lord Shaftesbury’s sister and who is now on his
way out with his wife to visit the Brasseys, is
really named Hogg. He is the son of that
M'Garel Hogg who was at the head of the crooked
old ‘ Board of Perks ’ in London before the Demo-
cratic County Council was established. Maghera-
morne is a more mouth-filling name than Hogg,
though perhaps not quite so descriptive.
“ On the occasion of the recent Brassey visit
to the Western district, the school children, at a
place through which the vice-regal party was to
drive on its way to the station of a local
squattocrat, were drawn up in line outside the
school-house to yawn ‘ Gorsave ’ as the carriage
passed. They were all there, expectant and jubi-
lant, waiting for H.E., and presently a cloud of
dust signalled the approach. When the wag-
gonette drove by ‘ Gorsave ’ was poured out with
gusto, followed by three cheers. Then the school
broke up. But presently a fat little man on a
bicycle, dressed in a grey suit, very dusty, and
looking scarlet, tired and hot, rode along. There
was no ‘Gorsave’ for him—only jeers and cries of
‘ Gee up, fatty ! ’ and so on. But lo ! the little fat
man was Baron Brassey, who had elected to ‘ bike ’
after the vehicle.
“ Flashlight portraits of the interiors of churches
and houses of entertainment are now much in
evidence in Melbourne shop-windows. A very
fine view of the inside of the Princess’ Theatre,
taken on a recent Saturday night, has been bought
by a complainant in a divorce-case, for collateral
evidence, as a respondent and co-respondent are
disclosed therein sitting next to one another. One
of the parties had represented to his wife that he
was in Bendigo that night, and if she had not seen
the photograph she might have believed him.
“If the society women who rolled up bare*
necked to hear young Tom Brassey orate on the
anniversary of Trafalgar at Melb. Town Hall,
could have tried the young gentleman for obtain-
ing their presence on false pretences they would
have sentenced him to sit and listen for a week to
a phonographic repetition of his own drawl.
Instead of good breezy stuff about Trafalgar, which
everybody expected, young is simply
an aggravated version of old Tom—merely flung
at his hearers blocks of statistics about the
Empyah. The women wanted no figures about
trade ; they wanted gory flapdoodle about Nelson,
with perhaps some hints on his relations with
Lady Hamilton, just to give spice to the historical
dry facts. But the Brasseys are hopeless cases.
They appear to have sucked in figures with their
mother’s milk, and the complaint comes out upon
them in • tabulated carbuncles and statistical
rashes.”

CLEO.

Many will be grieved to hear 'that the
vivacious “Handsome Charley” Lett, erstwhile
the idol of the Sydney women and the envy of
the men, is dead ; it is privately cabled that he
succumbed on 2nd Oct. to effusion of blood on the
brain For years Mr. Lett, who married a
daughter of old “Bobby” Towns, had lived in
London, where he did sundry journalistic work,
including some for The Bulletin. He was a year
or two ago appointed curator of the N.S.W.
Court at the Imperial Institute. Mr. Lett will
be much missed from London by Australian
visitors of his generation.

The rumored retirement of Justice* iruies,
iow that his pension-date is due, brings up the
.act that Australian Judges, if they don’t retire
and claim pension before the due date, seldom fail
to get on the list as soon as the clock strikes.
English Judges work on, oblivious of this time-
limit, and take pensions only when mentally or
physically played out.

So would Lyne, D.C.L.

Mr. Archibald Colqulioun Fraser, ex-
Secretary of N.S.W. Justice Dept., died last
Saturday, aged 64. After many years of service,
he found himself dismissed on pension from his
post without any stated reason and without a
moment’s warning. The man was wrapped up in
his office, and the indignity broke his heart—“l
do not care,” he said to more than one friend,
“how soon I die.” He began practice as a soli-
citor, but within a few weeks was stricken with
paralysis, from which, after lingering a while, he
died. Than Mr. Eraser, no Australian citizen
had or deserved more friends. He came of an
eminent Highland family

Some men are corn to be unluckj. few
years ago a lowers man, named Little, infected
with the glorious doctrine of Socialism in Our
Time, joined Lane’s New Australia movement.
After a wearisome battle he abandoned Paraguay
and obtained work at Buenos Ayres, losing two
fingers in a winch accident. Worked his passage
to London and landed there penniless. Returned
finally to the Towers, obtained work at the Queen’s
Central, and within a few weeks lost his life by an
accident. Verily Fate plays it down low on some
men.
Ex-Primate Barry, Sydney’s old aproned-
exhorter, is now waging war against the massage-.
dens of the West End of London. In the Parish of
St. James, perhaps the most crested portion of the
modern Babylon, Dr. Barry professes to have
ferreted out dens of massage infamy. The authori-
ties play the game of “not minding,” which the
Bishop attributes to the lofty station and great
social influence of the massaged votaries.
Col. Haultain, otherwise “the blue-tail
fly,” who was once Defence Minister in Maori -
land, had notoriously never been in action in his
life. Col. Tom M.‘Donnell, who ranked with Von
Tempsky as an absolutely fearless Maori fighter,
once reported to Haultain that he had been fired
on by some Maori rebels near Wanganui. “Ah
yaw shuah it wasn’t blank cartwidge ? ” asked
Haultain, with exasperating indifference. “Can’t
say, sir,’’said M Donnell, looking at him fair in
the eye, “/never was under blank cartridge fire
in my life.”

The Bishop of Melanesia appears to be St
large-minded prelate who can make allowances.
Deferring the other day to the massacre of an
Austrian baron and his party in one of the Solo-
mon Islands, he stated that he didn’t look upon it
as an act of treachery at all, but as simply part of
what might be called “the national policy” to
drive back hereditary enemies from the coast.
Bishop, your hand ! This is the policy of The
Bulletin with regard to the Chinese and other
invading aliens. Only this paper, being partially
civilised, is not so thorough as the Solomon Is-
’anders.

Allen and Allen, the Sydney firm of
solicitors, go back to the beginning of Australian
law. Grandfather Allen was the first Australian
admitted to practise as a solicitor. At that time,
barristers and solicitors were all one, and, when a
jury needed persuasion, Allen donned wig and
gown and called his fee a honorarium. At other
times, he worked in his bare bald head and charged
the inescapable 6s. Bd. This Allen held the chair
of N.S.W. Leg. Council for some years. His son,
Wigram, afterwards held the Speaker’s chair in
the Leg. Assembly, but stuck closely to law busi-
ness, though his father was W. C. Wentworth’s
chief opponent when the separation of the legal
branches was under discussion. There are nume-
rous young Allens growing up, so that an Allen
is likely to be on the roll when Australia cries “ A
go !”
Yet another old Sydney Reform Club
member has passed away in the person of Mr. C,
A. Goodchap, ex-Commissioner of N.S.W. Rail-
ways, who, in his prime, retired upon a pension of
just under <£looo a year. Mr. Goodchap, whose
name well-expressed his character, was of English
birth. He came to N.S. W r ales in his youth, and
by his tact and ability reached the top of the
ladder in the Railway Department He could have
had one of the new Commissionerships, but pre-
ferred retiring to accepting a secondary position.
With officers, men, and the public at large he was
unfeignedly popular. Some years ago his grit
enabled him to battle through a long and terrible
illness, from which, to all appearance, he had
thoroughly recovered; and when, but a week
or two ago, he went to see his sick friend, A. C.
Fraser, the latter said : “ They tell me I’m dying,
Goodchap—but you, why you look good for
another 20 years.” Notwithstanding which,
Goodchap preceded Fraser to the grave by a day.
Dr. M‘Laurin, M.D. (Edinburgh), M.A.,
and L.L.D. (St. Andrews), and M.L.C. (N.S.W.),
the newly-elected Chancellor of Sydney Univer-
sity, is a 61-years-old Scotsman, and one of Aus-
tralia’s few scholarly Conservatives. His life’s
training has imbued his mind with Draconian
traditions ; in ’5B he went from the e 1 as deal school-
ing of the University, and the severe doctrines of
ts medical profcsrcis to the inexorable discipline
Dr. M‘ LAURI N, the new Chancellor of
Sydney University.
Photo, by Crooke, Edinburgk.
of the navy. And, becoming ship’s surgeon at
the impressionable age of 23, and serving in that
capacity for thirteen years, he became moulded in
the principles of inflexibility. He says: “Aneasy
ship is an unhappy siiip; but a hard ship is happy.”
His ideal of government is reached when the laws
are so severe and so effective that the man who
obeys is perfectly safe, and the man who disobeys,
perfectly unsafe. “ When I was ship’s surgeon an
offender against the ship’s discipline could either
be put to death or receive whatever other punish-
ment court-martial might deem justifiable.” Of
flogging he says, “Only bad characters were flogged
in the navy, and the culprit always deserved each
lash of his punishment. On the Marlborough we
had 1140 hands, and they were not all angels.
One virtue of flogging is that, while it gives a man
an unpleasant experience which he won’t forget
for some time,' it allows you to utilise his ser-
vices in a day or two; he isn’t doing
nothing for a month in prison. The
severest flogging ever I saw was not administered
by the hand of authority, but was given by the.
crew themselves to one of their number. One
very old subordinate officer told me ‘ a man is not
a man until he’s been flogged.’ One of the punish-
ments inflicted when 1 was in the navy was to
make an offender toe a line, in the presence of the
crew, and swallow his daily allowance of rum
with six parts of water instead of three.” Dr.
M‘Laurin settled in Sydney in ’O9 : he has since
then always been a member of N.S.W. Board of
Public Health ; he became a member of Sydney
University senate in ’B3. He is one of the Uni-
versity’s examiners in medicine. In ’B9 he was
chosen by Dibbs as an M.L.C. The doctor is a
most excellent speaker, and full of pawky humor
of the James Service sort. As a practitioner, his
keen medical instinct and strong common sense
have won him the respect of the profession, and
the confidence of many patients. But let us thank
Heaven that Dr. M‘Laurin and those who think
with him can’t politically boss Australia. For
where you find one high-toned, clean-handed,
alert-minded Tory such as he, you find a hundred
moneyed hogs.

of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-

a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Warrnambool Murray, in Yic. Assembly :
“ He was certainly in favor of the reduction of the
number of members of Parliament —(loud Opposi-
tion cheers) —by the abolition of the Upper House.”
(Loud Ministerial laughter and cheers).
For the financial year ended June, 1896,
New South Wales raised £1,825,000 by customs
duties, and Victoria £1,733,000. Which of the
two is supposed to be the great Freetrade province,
by the way? Vue Bulletin really begins to
forget.
The fact that N.S.W. Legislative Assem-
bly has passed a Bill abolishing capital punish-
ment, and that the judgment of the Legislative
Council is not yet known in respect of that Bill,
morally justifies the Executive in suspending the
law in the Dederers’ case.
A member of Vic. Assembly states that he
has closely examined the Leg. Council electoral
rolls and constituencies and finds not more than
150 eligible men in all the province who are pos-
sible candidates for the Upper House as the law
now stands. Eligibility in this connection, of
course, means length of purse.
The promoters of Tasmania’s latest grid-
ironing railway grab —the bars of the said grid-
iron cover 500,000 acres —are “William John
Clarke, Baronet ; Charles Myles Officer the
elder; Norman Armytage ; the Honorable Agar
Wynne; William Hordern ; David Brown ; and
Charles Myles Officer the younger.”
That noisy Tory, Ebenezer Wai’d, M.L.C.,
of S.A., recently wrote to the Register proclaim-
ing his solemn intention to immediately depose
Kingston and his “ motley crowd.” “ The ques-
tion is,” says Ward, “when shall the necessary
war begin ? ” This has already been the question
with the Stomach party for three long years.
Champion asks for suggestions practical
enough to prevent the exodus from Victoria to
W.A. and South Africa. If the Victorian work-
ing men are lions led by asses, as H.11.C. once
asserted, the exodus ought to be a good thing for
Victoria. Or is the army of lions emigrating on
its own while the leaders stay at home and bray ?

N.: “ Mistake to fetch out the bludgeon
to attack Royalty. The way is to sap and mine,

A Danish apothecary has forwarded a cure
for the rabbit plague. He sent it to the Governor
of Victoria. “My council is,” wrote the un-
called-for specialist, ‘ get as quickly as possible a
great lot of strong and healthy fox’s, both male
and female, and let them loose in the bush.” As
if Victoria has not already enough “ fox’s.” And
this at a time when Australia is talking about an
export trade in frozen poultry !
N.S.W. Labor-members have now as fine
a chance as they are likely to get of demonstrating
the “ squeezing ” ability which they proclaim to
be their prime virtue. Ten minutes would see the
“ Free ” Education Bill through the Assembly,
but Premier Reid, for several reasons, declines to
afford the time. The Bill has been read twice,
and if the session closes without its being finished
off Labor’s “squeezing ” power will be deservedly
discredited.
The Vic. employment registry offices will
shortly be “dealt with.” A Bill is before Parlia-
ment under which all such offices must be licensed.
License-holders must be people of good repute,
and their premises must be approved by the
Inspector of Factories. The fees to be charged
will be fixed by Government; the scale of fees is
to be conspicuously posted up in the office, and
there are heavy penalties for overcharges, or for
running unlicensed offices. This looks tolerably
right, but there is one thing wanting, as a look at
the New York State law will show. In New York
the fees are fixed by law, the registry-office keeper
is licensed, and no person who,can’t stand inspec-
tion can go into the business. Also, when the
registry man takes a fee from an applicant he must
kind him employment within one month or return the
money. That last proviso is the one which is most
needed in Australia.
Members of N.S.W. Legislative Assembly
should not forget in the hurry of breaking-up for
the holidays that the Upper House has three times
passed measures for reducing the number and
emoluments of the Public Works Committee, and
that the Lower House has each time burked or
evaded the Bill by going into recess.
