When the fate of a Ministry—Freetrade
or Protectionist—is under vote in N.S.W. Legis-
lative Assembly, between 100 and 120 members
almost always find their seats. When the aboli-
tion of capital punishment was put to the vote
last week only 27 of these members cou’d be found.
H. : “ Apply the sexagenarian Civil Ser-
vice retrenchment rule to politicians. After 00
there is not \\ per cent, of a Liberal left amid

Parkes, and the like, always described as the ordy
true friend of the people.”
One of the sights in the N.S.W. country
districts now is the old primitive team which
humps goods from Sydney as far out as Dubbo
and Mudgee and Goulburn, and does it below
railway rates. The team has never been quite ex-
tinguished by the railway, but at present it is
having a revival. Men are cheap and horses are
cheap, and imported feed is cheap, which helps to
account for it. Still the spectacle of a huge, old-
fashioned dray facing the awful gradients of the
Blue Mountains as it carries a load of Californian
flour out back seems to suggest that there is some-
thing wrong with the country’s economy.
THE DEDERERS, convicted of the murder, for gain, of an Indian hawker at Boggabri, N.S.W.
A speaker in Vic. Parliament “hoped that
poor and aged selectors would not be troubled by
the Lands office for their rent, as they were
within measurable distance of departing from
this life for a land of greener pastures.” “ Let us
hope,” interrupted Deakin, “ that they won’t find
the green pastures all monopolised. ” Considering
the number of pious Australian squatters who have
pegged-out the matter is very doubtful.
The attempt of Griffith, M.L.A., to make
education free as well as compulsory in N.S.W.,
has apparently come to naught. The school fees
bring in £70,000 a year, and Treasurer Reid
alleges that he can’t afford to part with this money
on any terms. He can’t afford free education ;
but he can afford free newspaper-postage.
HE HLLD THE JOKER.
PARSON : “It is a judgment of Heaven.”
COCKY (who has been burnt out twice): “But I euchred it this time.”
PARSON: “My good man, you cannot euchre the Almighty.”
COCKY: “ Can’t I! I had the darned, place insured.”
Djir Bulletin, —You state that for the
first time the item of hangman’s salary does not
appear in this year’s estimates for Q. It has been
placed, with grim and unconscious humor, in the
Suspense Account. — Spectator.
Another version of the Age of Consent. In
S.A. Parliament a clause in the Licensed Vic-
tuallers Bill penalises parents for sending children
under 15 years of age for liquor. The unhappy
parent who has no children over the prohibited
age will now have to totter to the pub. himself
when he wants a drink, or else perish miserably of
thirst.
C. : Probable that the official ballot-box
used by N.S.W. Lands Department, in ballot-
ing for the determination of applications, can
be so manipulated by turning a knob that the
first marble dropped in will be the first marble to
drop out, no matter how much the box is shaken
and revolved after the other marbles are put in.
By turning the knob, the first marble sticks fast
in a slot, and only a violent bang underneath the
bottom can make it jump up and out. What a
rosy chance of well-paid fraudulent balloting for
a man “in the know ” !
The measure making eight hours the legal
maximum day’s work in mines and factories was
rejected in Maoriland Leg. Council by two votes.
Reasons given : (1) Eight hours is the regular
working day already ; (2) It is evidently going to
continue so ; (3) Therefore the measure is unneces-
sary, also harmful, autocratic, and an outrage on
the liberty of the subject. In other words, the
Tory party first alleged that it made no difference
anyhow, and then got into a frenzy of opposition
that nearly tore the roof off.
Gov. Brassey’s son, just now stumping
Melbourne on the Imperial Federation ticket
thinks that “ when the colonies no longer require
such a large proportion of their resources for the
development of their territory it will not be un-
reasonable to hope that the colonial taxpayer will
stand shoulder to shoulder with the British tax-
payer in bearing the common burdens.” This,
however, is one of the parts of the Hon. T. A.
Brassey’s sermon which doesn’t excite tremendous
applause.
The N.S.W. Government is pushing
through a Referendum scheme to settle deadlocks
between the two Houses. The great deadlock
which looms ahead is over the question of reform-
ing the Council on Democratic lines, and if the
Referendum scheme can be passed then the way
is practically clear. If it is rejected, then it will
be time for Premier Reid to call on Gov. Hamp-
den and state that he wants to send ten more
Liberals to the Council for the purpose of getting
this measure through, and read Neild’s poetry to
him till he gives in.
The proposal now before Tas. Parliament
whereby a syndicate seeks to snavel a thirtieth of
the province and to establish a pre-emptive right
to an area equalling a sixteenth of the total
area of the Land of Sleep a Lot, opens up a ques-
tion of federal interest. Has any province the
right to enter into an agreement which will affect
all the partners to the federation without consult-
ing those prospective partners ? If federal rights
were well considered before union there might be
less trouble over State rights after the wedding.
M‘Lellan/the Dove of Ararat, had a burst
of his old-time energy, the other day, when the
One-Adult-One-Vote Bill was before Vic. As-
sembly. “I would scrape the Ministry off the
benches with a rusty hook”—“ drivelling idiots ”
hairy-faced harridans ” “ dogs ” “ she-
wolves”—“ brazen-faced agitators ” were the most
audible parts of the old man’s oration. But no
one really listens to the Dove of Ararat nowadays.
He has been “ agin everything ” for so many years
in exactly the same wild cuss-language that he is
regarded now as an uninteresting crank, and the
Assembly goes to sleep under his wildest blare of
frenzy.
The point of view makes all the difference.
The Victorian railways lost .£312 last year through
carrying local coal at half cost to encourage the
industry, and the Government promises to go on
doing so for a while longer. And Sydney Tele-
graph, in last Monday's leading article, says that
this is “ a striking illustration ” of how Protection
taxes the masses to support “ influential producing
cliques.” Last year the N.S.W. postal depart-
ment lost about £70,000, a great part of it through
carrying newspapers gratis— which is a lot worse
than carrying them at half rates only—to support
the influential clique which produces the Daily
Telegraph, and what this is a striking illustra-
tion of isn’t even mentioned.
Even the placid, subservient Westralian
Legislature has begun to kick. A little while
ago it actually passed a motion in which it
declared that the action of the British Govern-
ment—in refusing to let Westralia steal the per-
centage of its revenue set aside by the Constitu-
tion Act for the benefit of the province’s wretched
aboriginals, who are rapidly being starved and
murdered into oblivion—was “subversive of the
rights of the people and not calculated to in-
spire confidence.” And that, coming from Perth,
is about the same as if N.S.W. Assembly had
voted that the British Government was a degraded
incendiary, and unfit to carry offal to an illegiti-
mate bear.
Maoriland Parliament dissolved a few dayo
ago, and the question whether Digger Seddon’s
Ministry is good for a third term of office will
probably be settled next mouth. At latest advices
the committee of the Lower House, like that of
the Council, had acquitted the Government of all
the charges made against it re the bank amalga-
mation and the bursting of Treas. Ward, so the
Ass party has lost a convenient weapon. Also,
the State Land Bank is getting along well, so,
apart from the Cold Tea question, it will appar-
ently be necessary for the Tory party to fight the
election on the old-fashioned lines, which consist
in baldly stating that the other crowd is a coarse
hog and a swindler and keeps a harem, and is
given to secret drinking.
The chief interest at the coming M.L.
general elections centres in the Hawkes Bay elec-
torate. Three years ago the alleged “Captain”
Russell, Opposition leader, was elected by a
majority of only 70 votes. The electorate was
ripe for a victory for Govt, candidate Reardon,
when the bull-headed son of John Tanner (the
father of Hastings) nominated his father as Inde-
pendent Liberal during the old man’s voyage from
England. This split the Liberal-Labor vote and
gave the great champion of the Chows and cheap
labor a fresh lease of political life. The moral
effect of the defeat of Russell would be tremendous
—for 17 years he has been the immovable rock of
M.L. Conservatism and the rallying-point of the
reactionary forces; and if “Dictator” Smith
won’t undertake to smash him, the Liberals should
call in a Minister to wipe out the last representa-
tive of the red-blanket system and detested Con-
tinuous Ministry.

Capt. Wallington sent a written complaint
to the editor of S.A. Advertiser because one of
the reporters of the paper, who was kept waiting
in the orderly-room at Govt. House, had the
audacity to smoke a cigar there. It wasn’t one of
Capt. Wallington’s cigars either.
The bitter cry of the Melbourne councillor,
who considered himself chowsed out of his turn to
be Mayor, was. rather excusable under the circum-
stances. Next year is expected to rain knight-
hoods upon all sorts of people who send congratu-
latory addresses to Her Gracious, and the current
Smellbourne Mayor is likely to get a drop of glory
from the shower.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The V.R.C. Derby next Saturday must be very
interesting, seeing that the three-year-olds have a more
than ordinary chance of playing principal parts in the
Cup race. The older horses last year were below the
average in “class” and stamina, and youngsters took
them down in big handicaps. Auraria, after finishing
third for the Derby, won Melb. Cup. Wallace, who
carried off the Derby, subsequently won the Sydney Cup,
and only missed the Australian Cup by half-a-head from a
feather-weighted veteran. This year’s lot of old horses
seems exceptionally poor in quality. The crack three-
year-olds of 1895 haven’t trained on, and the top-weight
of the candidates still remaining in the Cup handicap at
time of writing is Preston, a second-class animal. Yet
Preston has a slight show on paper. Probably, then, the
race for this year’s Derby will give such strong tips for
the handicap event that the betting-market will be
revolutionised, and The Bulletin notions, which go to
press a week previous to the Cup, will be despised and re-
jeeted by the man who wrote ’em.
•
Anything that may happen to capture or get
near to capturing the Derby—in smart style and fast
time—is likely to start a good favorite for the Cup. If
the writer had already seen either Resolute, Charge,
Sabretache, Coil, or Reka win the Derby decisively, he
would tip the victorious one to run first or second for the
Cup. Newhaven and The Officer he reckons as somewhat
less likely to stay the two-mile course, although nothing
has been proved against them on that point. Meanwhile,
how is the Derby winner to be spotted beforehand ? New-
haven may have been a brilliant horse in the early spring,
when, by the way, the sensational chestnut didn’t plainly
show that he can stay 1* mile under Derby weight. New-
haven’s performance in the Caulfield Guineas was moder-
ate, and he is suspected of having “ gone off ” since then,
so The Bulletin doesn’t much fancy this onetime even-
money favorite for the Derby. The Officer will undoubt-
edly take the Blue Ribbon, if he can stay the Derby
distance. Of Resolute, Charge, Coil, Reka, and
Sabretache it may be expected that they can all
go H mile without getting “ baked”—the question
is which of them can raise the best spurt at the
end of the gallop. Coil has, perhaps, improved
of late more than Charge, Sabretache or Reka, and Reso-
lute, on his Caulfield Cup form and appearance, is a fine
3 T oungster with poor feet. Moreover, he was greatly
fancied by the stable for the Caulfield Cup, and W. R.
Wilson, a not unduly sanguine owner, considered him “ a
dead bird.” It is unfortunate that Resolute’s chance for
the Derby (and Cup afterwards) will have to be measured
by his feet. Should this imposing colt get along all right
until Saturday, he will be The Bulletin’s champion for
the Derby. Next to the St. Alban’s colt, this journal
swears by Charge, Newhaven, The Officer, and Coil, in
the order given.
• • • • • *•
The first four horses in the Cup will—to the
best of The Bulletin’s guessing belief—include, at least,
a couple of three-year-olds. Take Resolute, Charge, New-
haven and Coil as the most likely ones to supply that
couple, and judge from the Derby running which three-
year-old will be best worth backing for a win and a
‘ place. The Bulletin expects Resolute and Charge to
beat Newhaven, Con, and The Officer in the Cup, pro-
vided that they (the aforesaid Resolute and Charge) have
run among the first four for the Derby. In fact, if Reso-
lute were endowed with ordinary hoofs he would be
worth backingstraightout for the Cup and for the “double.”
Charge, who goes well in his gallops, is certainly worth a
“ place” investment, anyhow.
. . : . . .
Touching the main body of Cup candidates, 4-
year-old and older, some sort of decent argument, ancient
or modern, might be advanced in favor of Devon, The
Skipper, O’Trigger, Marusa, Dreamland, The Merry Boy,
Damien, Trentham, The Chevalier, Nada, Hopscotch!
Disfigured, Bloodshot, and Broken Hill. Something
might be said for every animal in the race, but of the
twenty, or so, possible runners that have not been named
in the foregoing discourse the writer can see no urgent
reason for mentioning any save Tir6, Pivot and Canoona.
The two latter are mentioned merely for the sake of re-
marking that they are bound, on “paper,” to finish close
together, probably half-way between the head and tail of
the field.
Of the selected fifteen (including Tire, who is a
three-year-old, but not a Derby colt) the writer now pro-
ceeds to discard seven—viz., Dreamland, Damien, Dis-
figured, Bloodshot, Broken Hill, Tirfi, and Hopscotch,
of which lot Mr. Bill Long’s large and speedy representa-
tive seems the most likely to run a great race. Hop-
scotch can gallop, and he is very well just now, ana he
ought to finish among the first six. But inasmuch as his
“form” has been brilliant only up to lj miles, and his
impetuosity denotes the non-stayer, Hopscotch shall not
carry the writer’s ss. Bloodshot may win a race at the
V.R.C. meeting, but not the Cup. Disfigured is short of
condition; Dreamland, although looking well just now,
may be expected to fail through weakness rather than
want of pluck ; and Broken Hill—well, all Adelaide swears
by Auraria’s stable companion without attempting to find
reasonable justification for the oath. Broken Hill is a
beautiful horse to contemplate, but his shows in public for
the past two years have been contemptible. Allowing
that his bad leg, which is now strong again, kept him from
being properly wound up until lately, we have to con-
sider that his very best performance would represent
Portsea at lOst. 71b.' in the Cup. That doesn’t look very
tempting, although Portsea was a little wonder at the
time when he gave Broken Hill 371 b. and ran him to a
head. Mr. David James’s seven-year-old may be another
resurrection case, like Cremorne, but itisn t w if.® t( U a ®
resurrection for granted. Should Broken Hill run for
the Hotham Handicap on Saturday his Cup chance will
show itself.
_ ,’ T _ ’ ", . ', . ,
Devon and Nada were both placed in the “4
Cup. They are looking hard and bright this spring and
must be respected in spite of their recent indifferent
running. Skipper, Chevalier, Trentham, O’Trigger,
Marusa and The Merry Boy are stayers, more or less cer-
tain, with some “ form ”to recommend them. None of
them are first-class animals, judged by their performances
up to date, but they are the most likely of all the old
ones, as far as The Bulletin can see, hear, or suspect,
Perhaps Marusa (who has been patiently kept for a coup)
and The Merry Boy will prove the best of these, yet not
good enough to beat the best of the youngsters. The
Bulletin’s tip for the Cup will be Resolute if he wins the
Derby or finishes close behind the winner. Marusa,
Charge and Merry Boy may beat the others. Sensible
people will wait and back the first two horses in the Mel-
bourne Stakes and Derby for places in the Cup.

Without a smile on its pink countenance, Melb.
Sportsman says :—“ The victory of Cremorne has narrowed
down the up-to-date successful pickers of our £lOO Quad-
rliple Coupon (Caulfield Guineas, Caulfield Cup, Mari-
byrnong Plate, and Melbourne Cup) to one.” The lucky
one has Bohemian Girl and Newhaven going for him in
the other two portions of the £lOO Quadruple, so the
Sportsman’s up-to-date risk is about half-a-sovereign. A
bookmaker would bet 200 to 1 against the double—Bohe-
niian Girl and Newhaven. If the guesser had backed his
own selection for twopence—2d. on The Officer would
have given him 16d. to put upon Cremorne-he would
have done much better than by investing a 2d. stamp on
the envelope which carried his coupon to the Sportsman
office. Regrets, however, are useless,

Trotting shows better prospects in N.S. Wales
than it has for many a long day. Lillie Bridge has its
weekly trot for the small fry, and Brighton Beach its
fortnightly meet; Canterbury Park is giving an occa-
sional flutter ; Dubbo has a big show on 9th Nov.; and
now Mr. “Johnny” Deeble is about to give some good
prizes at Brighton, for which Mr. “Fitz” Buckland pro-
misGS to scud a. team to Sydney.

South Africa is setting aside a day of
humiliation and prayer to root out the rinderpest.
Australia should promptly counteract this by
appointing a day of thanksgiving and praying for
its continuance, as the South African meat-supply
will soon run out and give our meat a show.
Up South, the other day, a well-known
citizen was before the Court charged with
various financial transgressions, confidence tricks,
&c. It was alleged that accused was in the habit
of assuming and signing the letters J.P. after his
name “in order to inspire confidence ”! ! ! What
a simple world he must have lived in !
In some of the Yic. State schools pupil
are now taught to write.vertieally. This is to keep
them from getting strabismus and contortion of
the spine— suj>posed to be frequently acquired in
mastering the present oblique style of writing.
Also, Minister Peacock is stated to be so pleased
with the results of his sewing-for-boys innovation
that he intends to introduce laundry work for boys
into the State school curriculum. Peacock evi-
dently believes that the New Man of the future
will have to do all the housework.
An irate Sydney alderman : “They had
had to furnish Govt, with a good many particulars
of late : one parcel cost Bd. in stamps, another Is.,
a third 6d., and so on. If the cook at Govern-
ment House can send his correspondence free, I
don’t see why this Council should not.”

The energy of half-a-dozen very thin, pale
sad, dejected small boys and girls drumming a
Melb. suburb in quest of broken victuals excited
attention recently. Investigation disclosed they
were the family of an enterprising poultry farmer
living some miles from town, who fed his stock on
the scraps collected by the alleged doleful orphans,
and was doing rather a good thing in fresh eggs
and spring chickens. Another bounty-fed in-
dustry !

At the opening of term in N.S.W.
Supreme Court, all the judges present regretted
the loss of W mdeyer, J., but not one of them had
a word of welcome for Cohen, J., who takes his
seat Ihe Bulletin will not say his place. At-
torney-Gen Want, for the Bar, both regretted
and congratulated, which made the omission of
their Honors more significant. Also, the Court’s
neglect to welcome Judge Cohen (it had no word
of welcome for him when first he took his seat)
was further accentuated by the ostentatious cor-
diality with which it congratulated Mr. Walsh on
his appointment as Prothonotary.
• r M^’ or °f N.5.W.,” now raging
m London over the neglect of Australia to send
wreaths for the Trafalgar anniversary celebration,
is a very new major indeed. So far as the N.S.W.
public is aware, the poet-warrior, up to the
date of his leaving Australia the other day, only
wore uniform once-on the occasion of a smoke-
concert given to welcome the. new Commandant.
Mr. -Neild had taken part in promoting a new
corps, the St. George’s Rifles, and at the concert
aforesaid he appeared wearing a sample of the
uniform selected for the corps, in which he had
obtained a commission, and which, as a matter of
fact had no actual legal existence at the time, in
that its members had not been sworn-in.

The advertisement of the Australian Mutual
Provident Society, appearing in another column,
intimates to taose contemplating assurance on
their lives that the next division of profits will 1 e
made as at 31st December next, and that all poli-
cies issued before that date will share in the
amount to be then distributed among the part ici-
patmg policy-holders. The amount of cash divided
%“cngst the members for the year 1895 was
r ’ wlllcl ' 1S a larger amount of
profit for one year than has ever been realised
or divided by any other life office in the
British Empire. r l lie society’s prospectus, con-
taining the rates for the various kinds of assur-
ances granted by the society, may be had or
application, and it would be well for intending
assurers to obtain a copy and send in early appli-
cation for a policy, so as to reap the benefit of this
year s bonus.*

Bill Martin has written a touching letter to Thf
Bulletin with regard to his recent Adelaide trouble
Everyone knows what trouble there was between
M Donell and myself m Sydney ; and neither of us has
spoken to the other since then. This time, the trouble
occurred in the finish of the final of the half-mile hand;
cap. Coming into the straight M'Donell was leading
with me upon his wheel on the outside I was catching
him; tvvo kicks and I’d have been levei. But at that
instant, he swung out from the middle of the track and
I, in the effort to save myself from a nasty fall, went into
the fence, while M‘Donell’s pedal ripped several spokes
as" nuslald *'° AftlrTh 1 ; / hiswas as
as mustaia. After the finish, as I was going to pass him
Lv ° Ufc a^a * n * be moment I got abreast him. So’
to keep him away, I shoved out my hand. (William must
have ‘ shoved it out sudden ! ’ M'Donell’s impresdon at
the time was that it came out of a gun.) After this I
lodged a protest against M'Donell, and came before the
committee to uphold it, but not one word or si-n was
raised about a protest against me. The first I heard of
my disqualification was on Sunday, coming from church •
it left me used up in three seconds.” This is indeed’

As affording a forecast of this season’s racing
form, the Charity Cycling Meeting at Melb Cricket
Ground last Saturday was both interesting and useful
Jimmy Carpenter, winner of the Scott-Morton road-
race, was the hero of the day, carrying off both the T adv
rSTnd ( *3o)’) ’ and ISt a " d 2nd Class" Handicap
(£2<J), and he thus appears to have struck track as well as
road form. Kuhle won a heat with a magnificent spurt
that evidently used up all his dash, for he was badly
leaten in the final; and Pither’s high gear seems to have
lost none of its vigor during the winter. Several novices
allowed their honesty and conceit to get the better of
Ans 1 - 1 'w IS h ret i° n f a 'l d vvon by strcets > knocking out every
possible hope of a big handicap in the Austral and other
caA P ed n off ra th e p S ’firA e 0 HeS vf e ’ the St Kilda Inotballer,
earned off the first race he ever started in, in a style
which proclaims him a coming champion. Stevenson
another new man, won a mile heat by nearly 200yds. ’
When the men tailed out for the final of the
Lady Brassey Handicap, her Excellency herself took up a
position in the centre of the arena, and, grasping a
with both hands, screwed up her face, shut her eves tiVht
rnd pulled the trigger, whereat the’assembled J popuface
cheered vehemently at the brave woman. But themost
touching sight of all was after the race, when Carpenter
ill bare legs, too-tight-fitting knickers, and perspiration’
was led by some loyal person to shake hands with “ Sybil
De Vere ’’ Brassey. He seemed to feel his position keenly.
Parsons dealt it out effectually to his own
world’s grass-record for half-a-mile (lmin. 3 2-ssec.), re-
that quantity by 4 l-ssec. He took 29sec. to turn
off half the distance, as against 28sec., his previous best,
but accomplished the two laps in 59 l-ssee. At one corner
the pace failed, and when leaving the triplet at the finish
Parsons lost several yards ; so it is likely that, had all cir-
cumstances been favorable, he would have brought the
time below the 58sec. which Ziutmerman did on the Exhi-
bition asphalte.
Parsons claims to have grown half-an-inch in
height since leaving ’Frisco six weeks ago. He is now
sft. 6fin., and weighs in condition lOst. 81b.
John S. Johnson, the Yankee crack cyclist, pre-
sented Parsons with a big gold chronometer during his
stay in the States. The Australian had the portrait of his
best girl enamelled on the back. By the way, affaires de
coewr are understood to be not entirely disconnected with
Parsons’ quick return to this country.
Parsons has broken every record that he has yet
tried for.

There are no appeals from Chief Justice Mad-
den’s opinions on racing- matters, whatever there may be
about his legal decisions. The other day, when a witness
swore positively to a date (12th Jan., ’B9), because he had
pulled off a double at Elsternwick on that date, the Chief
was instantly down upon him to know the names of the
winners, when the witness replied—“ Nectarine and For-
saken.” The Court was satisfied and complimented the
witness on his veracity.

Mick Nathan, the Melb. boxing caterer, usually
“ deals out ” one of his characteristic little speeches to
the Victoria Hall audience on Saturday nights. After in-
troducing the pugs, on one occasion he remarked : “ This
here is going to be a straight go ! If there’s anv gammon
the lads won’t get the money. I tell them that'right here
to show that I like to depreciate my patrons.” Then
the “bashing” began.
One solitary London bobby ejected pug- Peter
Jackson recently from the Tivoli, and Peter’s passiveness
got him off with a fine of ss. only. When Mitchell and
olavin have their monthly “outings” the price averages
£lO each and hospital treatment for anything* over a
dozen “coppers.” Peter sticks to whisky at his own ex-
pense, the other two affect “fizz” at the expense of the
jays, and the results pan out apparently in their proper
proportions.

Stanbury has filled up his interviewers with
a fairly consistent storj'. His making no effort
after appealing for a foul is explained by Jim’s
being mistaken in being sure. Beach, it will be remem-
beied, when fouled on the Thames, made sure by coining’
in first with a swamped boat. And there was no doubt
about Beach’s being fouled, because his opponent’s bow
cut Beach’s leg Beach to-day is greatly surprised at
Stanbury s defeat. “He could row a mile as fast as ever
I could, says Beach.

The toughest slugging-matches yet seen in Melb
are for beer; the best pug. at the end of each round gets
a pint. A dog-chewing contest is a love-scene compared
to this sort of thing. r

her)

dona” :

boasting,
No longer stand accused of “blow,” the latest term is
“ coasting.”
When Edwin smiles in Angy’s eyes, and can’t misunder-
stand ’em,
The spoony pair, in biking phrase, are merely “ridin<>-
tandem.” 6
Flash drinking cads are “ geared too high,” a parent
stern is taking
“The high wheel” for his title, whilst a chaperone’s
pace-making.”
This cycle craze has bred a name for every brand of ass
type,
And I who write—a cheap machine—must call myself a
“gas-pipe.”

THE ADVANCE OF SCIENCE.
BUT FEW DISEASES TO-DAY THAT
CANNOT BE CURED.
A Well-known Physician Speaks of His Ex-
periments with one of the Most Noted Modern
Medicines.
(From the lowa State Register.)
A few years ago the list of incurable diseases
was a long one, but now, in the age of enlighten-
ment, nothing seems impossible. Diseases which
for years battled the best physicians in the world
set the chemists and wise men thinking, and, as a
result, many valuable remedies were discovered.
First among these remedies stands Dr. Williams
Pink Pills. This medicine was first compounded
by an eminent physician, who used it in his practice
for a. number of years with remarkable success.
Hundreds of reports are received of marvellous
cures effected by these pills. Among the most
noted reports is one from Dr. W. A. Wright, of
Thayar, la., who states that he considered Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People the grandest
remedy he had ever seen. The Register deter-
mined to investigate the report, and a reporter
called up the Doctor on the telephone. As good
luck would have it, he caught Dr. Wright at his
office, and he spoke as follows of his experience
with Pink Pills :
“ They certainly are of great benefit in nervous
diseases of all sorts which afflict both sexes. I re-
member two cases in particular—the ethics of my
profession prevent my giving names—in which the
results were almost extraordinary. In one case a
young man was afflicted with neurasthenia. He
is the son of a doctor who lives in Murray, lowa,
and at last, after his own father could not cure
him, or even give him relief, the poor boy almost
lost hopes of ever regaining even moderately good
health. He was weak and emaciated, his coun-
tenance pale and haggard, and, in fact, was a heavy
burden to himself. Medicines were given, but
they didn’t touch the right spot, and finally Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills were tried, and the immediate
change in his condition was remarkable. An im-
provement was noticed at once, and gradually he
regained his strength and health, until now he is a
strong, robust man, without an ill to bother him.
“ The other case I remember as one that was
very successfully treated was that of a young girl
of thirteen years of age who was suffering from
chorea, or, as more commonly known, the St.
Vitus’ Dance. For five years and a half she had
been treated in vain for this dreadful affliction,
which was gradually but surely taking her
life. When she came to me she was com-
pletely wrecked, and her condition was very
deplorable and pitiful. She was a mere skele-
ton, only weighed sixty-five pounds, couldn’t
feed herself, couldn’t sit on a chair by her-
self, and, in fact, was perfectly helpless. Well,
sir, I tried a number of good remedies, but no im-
provement came, until, finally, I administered
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. Soon there was a marked
improvement. Gradually she regained her health,
and now is completely cured and weighs one
hundred and fifteen pounds. I want you to under-
stand that this case was in its worst stage, and
pronounced incurable.
“I have,” continued the Doctor, “used these
pills in many other cases, and have always at-
tained successful results. These two cases I have
spoken of are most extraordinary. ‘ Wonderful ’
is a strong word, but it expresses the results
exactly. I have found the pills a grand cure for
locomotor ataxia, and in all nervous diseases they
certainly are very fine.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are obtainable from all
leading chemists, or from the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Coy., Queen’s Place, Sydney, who will for-
ward (post paid) on receipt of stamps or post order
L box for 2s. 9d., or \ dozen for 14s. 9d.

ERUPTIONS
On the Face, Cured by
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla
Remarkable Experience of Miss Dorothy
Maher, Fitzroy, Victoria, whose portrait
we are privileged to give below:
-
“I take pleasure in testifying to
the great benefit I derived from
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla. I suffered
from eruptions on my face of a very
irritating and vexatious nature.
For a considerable time I experi-
mented with various so-called blood
medicines, but without any allevia-
tion of my trouble. At last, your
famous Sarsaparilla being strongly
recommended to me, I began to use
it, and after taking two bottles it
was most gratifying to see and feel
the effect in allaying the irritation
and reducing the eruptions. When
I had used three bottles the erup-
tions disappeared altogether, with-
out leaving a mark on my face, and
I have never been troubled with
anything of the kind since.”

“ In every community there are to be found
men who fear to pass through any gate unless
somebody’s great-grandmother has passed through
it before them,” says Max Hirsch.
Two men had a contest, the other day, at
“ pushing down arms,” i.e., clasping hands with
elbows resting on the table. The beaten man felt
something snap, was taken to hospital, and the
inquest showed three fractures of the upper bone
of the arm, due to the spiral twisting to which the
winner had subjected it.
Oil of cedar is a splendid preventive of
mosquitoes and midges, wherefore some wise
Q’sland chemist might do worse than distil it from
the common Australian red cedar (Oedrela toona)
on a commercial scale. It is this antipathy of in-
sects to the oil that caused West Indian cigar-
manufacturers to select cedar for their box material.
The other day out hack a Chinaman was
being conveyed to the grave in a spring-cart which
also carried four live Chinamen, a baked pig,
boiled rice, and the other paraphernalia of a
Chinese funeral. Half way to the cemetery the
horse bolted and spilt the contents of the cart, the
corpse and live Chinamen included. The coffin
broke open, and the corpse fell out —which ghastly
circumstance did not prevent some alleged white
Eersons from taking a permanent loan of the
aked pig during the excitement.
H. : “ The English are surprised that they
could not astonish Chang. The reason is because
he appreciates the supremacy of the spiritual, the
moral and truly religious, over the material. Thus
he finds China beats England, and a mass of sin
is not to be extinguished by railways, telegraphs,
telephones, and electric-light, which, after all, he
doesn’t find to put out the sun’s eye. Same in
India : the people are intensely religious, and the
material English works are only travelling on
earth to reach the stars.”
Every now and again Sydney rock oysters
suffer from a plague which goes through them
even as Asiatic cholera wipes out Mecca pilgrims,
or smallpox devastates mulga blacks. The cause
is now ascertained by Prof. Haswell, of Sydney,
to be a very small annelid (worm) which forms
curious, discolored, more or less sinuous blisters on
the inner surface of the shell. A small blister
seems to do no more harm than a cold in the nose
in man, but a large blistered area kills as sure as
la grippe in a girl with lung-trouble.
Nanism (tendency to dwarfishness) has
been experimentally shown by de Yarigny to be
due to want of activity and movement in the
parent. This seems to some extent to explain why
large, fat mothers so often have undersized child-
ren, whereas little women continually on the move
have families both large in number and large in
height. Facts in this connection should be re-
corded by every country doctor—especially in Aus-
tralia, where a change in type from the Bull to the
Jonathan build is taking place under everybody’s
I eyes. Even policemen have noticed it.
Corpulent people should avoid, and thin,
weedy folk energetically devour, fat meats, starchy
foods, sweets, peas, parsnips, beet, carrots, thick
soups, sauces, cream, milk, cocoa, chocolate, and
similar foods. Vice versa, folk inclined to put on
weight should vigorously take, even as thin folk
should steer clear of, lean meat, poultry, game,
eggs, fish, green vegetables, salads, apples, pears,
tea, and coffee. So stout folk, who mostly run to
liver and kidney troubles, should avoid brandy,
rum, strong whisky, port, stout, sweet fruity wines,
and stick to whisky and tonic-water, gin well
diluted with natural carbonated water, light dry
wines ; the_ converse, of course, being indicated
for chlorotics, ansemics, and other weeds.

The recent wreck of a shop in Aucklan
(M.L.) by the bursting of a water-main, recalls
the vagaries of a hot mud-and-water geyser a *
M.L. Hot Lakes. One M‘Rae had just got his
newly-built pub. (wooden, of course) hi .e " V
tourists, when, about 10 p.m. on a Sunday, t .
hall-floor heaved and a geyser threw brahngnrad
all round. The company abandoned the ground-
floor, took the contents of the bar upstairs, and
settled down to enjoy themselves Abe deau
>uns ” were placed in three large clothes-baskets
on the balcony, and, when the baskets were filled,
Patsy Dooley, rouseabout, was told to take the
to the kitchen. Half-way down Patsy grew tired
and threw the bottles into the geyser, which made
a fearsome row and, about an hour later, flu g
most of them out with a tremendous roar ot dis-
gust. At daylight the geyser disappeared as sud-
denly as it had come, and MTiae took the ejected
botties, which were lined with a curious filigr ~
work of various colors, and sold them at a big
price as curios. For months after M Rae wen
round the district flinging bottles into the various
geysers to get them turned into objects of vertu ,
but all in vain. It was like the man of the Lost
Drink ” : he could never get a geyser to color his
bottles again.

Extracts from C. H. Bamford’s “ Day in
the Life of a Sewing-Machine Canvasser, read at
a recent Melb. smoke-night :
Full of confidence I stepped up boldly to the door of
the first house in a terrace of eight and rang the hell at
least I would have done so only there was not any bell to
ring, so I rang the knocker instead. The door opened a
little and a head peeped cautiously round the corner >(
“Good morning,” I said, “have you— No. Ihaven t,
and the door banged to. She hadn’t either, for she vas
the dirtiest-looking woman man ever beheld. Not to lie
deterred, after a short wait, changing the method of the
operation, I rang-at least, knocked again-a wait-a foot-
step -the door again cautiously opened, the same head
peeped out—“ Good morning, can I wo, you
can’t,” and the door slammed to. .
It is borne in upon me by this time that I can t, and so
I determine to try the next house. As Idoso I espy a
woman seated upon the balcony. I am so sure she has
overheard the previous conversation that out of veiy
bravado I determine to make her come down or die in the
attempt. Fixing myself m a favorable attitude and tak-
in.r a long breath, I work that knocker tor all it is worth,
then, after an ineffectual cannonade, pause for want of
breath, wait a moment, and then begin again. Still no
response. Just as lam about to make a third assault n
which I will either bring down the knocker, the old
woman, or the house, a voice from above says Did.you
knock ? ” With a fine, withering sarcasm I reply, Rid 1
knock? oh no, not at all,” as I step back, and finding it
evidently beyond my power to force the enemy to come
down, determine to attack from the garden.
“Goodmorning,” I begin—there is much virtue in your
good morning, it is always a politic introduction. Good
morning, can I sell you a sewing machine, one of the
finest in the universe, used by all the crowned heads of
this world and the other, being specially prepared to
withstand a warm climate. Our machines are not only
the eighth wonder, but they virtually embody the seven
others as well. We have 50,000 billion in circulation and
turn out a complete machine every second, that is »
minute, 1000 a day, 10,000 per week, 100,000 per month,
! 20 millions per year.” (This is not arithmetically correct,
but strict accuracy is impossible when you are striving
after dramatic effect.) “ Can I sell you one of our latest;
it is a sort of combination of bicycle and treadmill. The
charges are very moderate ; the agreement certainly does
say something about 2s. 6d. per week, but if you give the
collector eighteenpence during the first twelve months,
and one shilling or one and threepence every two years
afterwards, it will be all right. Can I sell you one ?
All this time she has been, leaning forward with her
chin resting in her bands eyeing me attentively. T)id
you knock?” she asks again. “Bah! I grunt in disgust.
As I turn to leave, a small boy says, “It amt.no use,
mister; she’s as deaf as a post.” There are six more
houses in that terrace, but I have had enough.

In the River.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a heavy, all-night downpour heavy
even for the ever-moist West Coast, where ram
is never prayed for. The three piners—Jones,
Sullivan and Oliver —listened to its patterings on
their tent and rejoiced. They had a cargo of logs
all ready for transport to the sea-board, but an
unusually dry spell had kept the river low. A
flood is the Tasmanian pine-cutter’s waggon,
railway, or lighter —the means by which he gets
his goods to market.
* * * *
Daylight saw it raining as consistently as
ever. The river roared along with a full pressure
of dirt-discolored water —sweeping away logs,
trees, and the varied flotsam and jetsam contri-
buted by the forests through which it took its
course. The men hurried an early breakfast, for
there was work ahead. They were soon at it
clearing and starting laggard logs ; pushing others
clear of the eddies into the run; and averting
threatened “jambs.” Wet, cold work yet ex-
citing for the spice of danger in it. There is a
fascinating wildness in the disorderly procession
of flood-borne logs. Their passage is fraught with
many uncertainties. Now arrested by low ex-
tending arms of the river scrub; now carried
forward by the smooth, swift fresh of the deep
reaches ; on, ever onward, to the mad swirl of
the rapids, over which they shoot erratically, now
hurled right out of the water, and again diving
into the depths; the noise of their contact with
the boulders resounding above the din of waters.
An ever-varying picture of man’s utilisation of the
forces of Nature to serve his ends.
• * * *
About mid-day the party decided to em-
bark in their punts and follow their timber. Rain
was still falling, and the river increasing in
volume. Securing their effects, away they went,
scarcely any pulling being necessary other than to
assist the steersman, whose post is no sinecure
when so much debris is awash.
All went well with them for about half of their
journey—the worst half—till they came to a rapid
at the foot of which a small island divided the
stream. Just as the boat was fairly in the swift
“ run,” at the top of the fall, she grated heavily
on an unnoticed submerged snsg—a sharp and
deadly obstacle which tore the thin planking of
the boat’s bottom as if it had been brown-paper.
In an instant she filled and rolled over, throwing
out her crew, who were swirled forward by the
furious rush of icy water—bruised against the
rocks, and half smothered. Oliver went down
stream for some distance, till at last he managed
to grasp some bushes and pull himself ashore.
The others effected a landing on the island, which
was but a few inches above water. The wrecked
boat was swept clear away.
* * * *
Oliver lay till he regained his breath. Then
he looked round for his companions, whom he had
not seen since the capsize ; he knew that neither of
them could swim. His coo-ees brought no re-
sponse—nor could they have been heard far above
the din of the river. He mounted a small promon-
tory, from which he could see up and down stream
for half a mile or so. On the little rocky islet, sur-
rounded bv the raging torrent, he saw his two
mates. Sullivan was busy about a small log
which some former flood had stranded, Jones lay
apparently inert and helpless—though not dead,
as Oliver first thought. Their position was pre-
carious, as the river was steadily rising. Sullivan,
working with all his energy to get the log clear of
the bushes, was too occupied to notice his mate’s
signals. It was evident that Sullivan meant to
use the log as a means of escape. He carried
his hapless, injured comrade to it and secured
him on the log with strips of shirting and their
leather belts. Oliver waited till the island was
nearly covered, then hurried down stream and took
up a position on a point where the river made a
bend, and stood ready to render assistance—pray-
ing the while that the log would swerve inshore.
* - * *
Presently he saw them coming Jones
stretched out but firmly lashed on ; Sullivan,
stripped to the waist, sat astride, swaying from
side to side in his efforts to keep the rolling thing
balanced, and maintaining his place by holding on
to something which girdled it. Fairly in mid-
stream, the log held its course. Oliver could do
nothing—what could be done ? If it had only run
into the eddy there was a chance, but things were
not ordered so. Sullivan saw him as they swept
past. With a smile on his face, he waved his hand
in farewell, shouting “ Good-bye, old chap. We re
bound ” The remainder was lost. There was
another rapid ahead, and Oliver watched them to
their doom. He saw the log roll over, as, with a
sudden shoot, the resistless waters tossed it on-
ward. He saw it a second or two later with but
one figure on it —him who was tied.
• * * »
At the river’s mouth the workers securing
the logs as they arrived were horrified to find one
bearing a ghastly, battered burden. Sullivans
plan had miscarried, and his last resource was
transformed into a hearse fpr Jones. Sullivan was
denied the poor privilege of “ decent burial,” as he
was seen no more.
The river kept its secret.
Hobart, Tas.
TAHUNE LINAH.
At Mokonui, on M.L. west coast, the other
day, the train started early and left a Maori sal-
vation lass behind. At the next station, a mile
and a half off, that identical wahine, barefooted
and bluchers in hand, leapt joyously into the
guard’s van with “ Hallelujah ! Kapai ! Caught
him that time.” She had chased the train across
the sleepers.
It is interesting to speculate what will be
the issue of the duel when the rapidly-approaching
tick and rabbit meet. They may possibly browse
one on the other, but it is much more probable
thev will become mutually acclimatised the
rabbit helping the tick as a beast of burden, and
the tick clearing the cattle off the rabbit-pastures.
An announcement that the Melb. Presby-
tery had decided to close Chalmers’ Church drew a
sentimental crowd of former attendants to a vale-
dictory service, on a recent Sunday night. Some
among the audience had been present at the
formal opening of the tabernacle 43 years ago,
and these people were visibly shaken up by then
youthful memories rather than by the moderate
sermon provided for their well-seasoned souls.
Yet there was a sense of hollowness about the
closing of Chalmers Church, inasmuch as it seems
pretty certain to re-open shortly under new
management. As a show, it couldn tbe compared
with the deconsecrating of a venerable gospel-mill
that is about to be pulled down. The writer has a
genuinely solemn recollection of a deconsecration
service at the old London church, in Bread-street,
where Milton had been christened. The church
had lost its congregation, and its site was wanted
for another warehouse, and the authorities—who-
ever they may have been, were going to wipe the
dingy edifice off the face of the narrow little
Bread-street. When they pulled it down, and
the slip of the churchyard came to be dug up,
the writer bought a human bone from one
of the workmen for the price of a pint
of beer, and duly soliloquised over the fragment of
forgotten mortality—but this is a digression. A
small crowd of decrepit softgoods potentates—the
street where Milton was born and christened is
full of ’em—came to hear the last sermon preached
by a parson as old as themselves. The octoge-
narian in the pulpit reminded these city pa-
rishioners of their younger days, when they were
comparatively poor and lived on the small pre-
mises which had since blossomed into big pre-
mises, the days long before they thought of
dwelling in gorgeous suburban residences, and
when they had “worshipped,” as the parson put
it, every Sunday within these rather shaky walls
where he was now addressing them for the last,
last time. And the bloated capitalists wept as
one man. Their hearts were touched, probably
by thoughts of how they once intended to give
away lots of money when their businesses in-
creased, and hadn’t done so after all. Anyway,
tears fell freely. The present writer s white-haired
boss was among the weepers, and as soon as he
had tottered mournfully back to his private office,
the p.w. made a fervent appeal for more salary
and didn’t get it. The old alligator said he was
too sad to discuss such matters.
The Salvos’ Error.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The hallelujah lass married the man because she
had converted him, and supposed she loved the re-
claimed lost sheep. The man married the woman
because he loved her. His was love; hers was
frenzy, such frenzy as all earnest religion is. But
the ill-assorted pair went the way of all such
couples. The good woman behaved as good
women do, and that is devilishly bad to the bad
man. Instead of being a crown to her husband
she was a shining-light and a tambourine in the
Army. She did not swear at him, she did not
drink, she threw no crockery ; but with due meek-
ness she nagged him off to Western Australia,
and when he was dry-blowing and drinking mud
she was yelling “Come to Salvation !” at street
corners.
Then came the find, and the find was for her and
for love. The love had never slackened on the
part of the man, and it never existed on the side of
the woman.
The return was not a triumph. The bank
deposit-receipt was not a heart-plaister, and the
welcome was only “How is your soul?” But
there was now a third party to be consulted—a
baby, in fact, and that ought to have been a bond
of love. I don’t believe the female thing loved
even that healthy squalling consequence.
What could the fellow do ? Follow her about to
the knee-drills and prayer-wrestles, or sit at home
and mind the kid? He was rich now, and could
pay a nurse ; but the other half said that was
pride and sinful. So he sat and went out to have
a drink. This was on the evening of a house-to-
house drink crusade. His wife crusaded him, up-
braided him, and held him up to scorn before the
man with the big mouth and drum, the girl with
the little mouth and trumpet, the captain with the
badly-fitting cap and sympathy, and herself with
her ghastly features and religion. The end was
rum. It began with a small beer and a smoke.
When the fire-bell rang in that one-horse Cen-
tral Queensland township the tambourine was
preparing a solemn warning without a drop of
mercy or love in it: a lot of hell and no chance of
heaven; a cry for repentance forgetting self-
righteousness. But there was no one to warn, not
even the baby. She could not well warn ashes.
SCOTTY THE WRINKLER.
The peculiar aroma of rum depends largely
upon the treatment of cane-juice in the boiling
house, and the action of a peculiar kind of bottom
yeast, known to chemists as “ 16T8 ” yeast. The
Trinidad growers are now specially using this,
and competing with the very finest Jamaica quali-
ties. Queensland makers should note this.
Maoriland Leg. Council has thrown out
the bill fixing a maximum legal rate of interest,
and the National Option Bill has gone overboard
along with it. Therefore, Seddon’s proposals for
further liberalising the Council will probably get
him many votes at the election, and destroy any
flickering chance the Ass party might otherwise
have possessed. The blank incapacity of the Ass
party is shown by its conduct on this occasion.
Its members are mostly placid old landlords, not
usurers, and the abolition of usury idea would
have done it very little personal injury. As for
national option, it should have, made it easier,
instead of throwing difficulties in the way, and
amended the bill so as to allow of the shutting, up
of every pub. in Maoriland on a bare majority.
Then if the Government objected it would have to
kill the measure itself, and lose the Cold Tea vote
for ever, while the Tory faction would get the
Cold Tea support without paying anything for it.
And if the Government accepted the amended
scheme, and the Cold Tea regime did arrive, and
the Seddon Government had to find the money to
supply the missing drink revenue, and burly
Richard of Kumara couldn’t get. a whisky for
love or money, and the great Public Thirst began
to rise in revolt against him —well, the Ass party
could lie back and brush the flies off itself with its
ears, and smile. It would have run Seddon into
the worst hole of his life, and left him there, and
it would have the credit, while he would have the
worry and the deficit and the anguished bald spot
on the top of his head and all the other miseries.
In the Bight.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a trifle of a thousand leagues of sea
And an immaterial million reefs or so
At her ending on the shore that lies a-lee ;
And from there a sweep clean to the Bole below.
There’s a vagrant gale that never yet was born,
Nor has died nor started, but has always blown; 1
And it whoops around a ship in noisy scorn,
And slings a thousand-tonner like a stone.
And that’s the Bight—the very large, important,
useful, great Australian Bight—
Four hundred feet of deck
Is no bigger than a speck,
And the coaster it's quite proudful on the big Aus-
tralian Bight.
The Bulimba there’s as good as any Rome ;
No more a coaster when she walks the Bight ;
She’s an ocean-goer once she lifts the foam
That runs a mile-race from the Borda light.
Damn all trusting to her Brisbane river berth !
Blast all warping on the Yarra’s greasy tide !
A skipper west feels that he owns the earth,
And he steams the Bight with large amount of
side.
On the Bight—the extremely wide and popular
and great Australian Bight.
The wave that makes her roll
Has been booked straight from the Polo,
Through a thousand leagues of ocean on the big
Australian Bight.
The coasting-ships will cross that bight for years
Though the wild-cat man may say the West is
done ;
The westward freights are answering his fears,
There’s a cargo-glutton underneath the sun.
Freight of stampers, freight of cattle, freight of
steel,
Ebbing westward through the day and by the
night,
Loaded downward from her decking to her keel,
’Twarn’t for nothing God Almighty dug the
Bight— .
The Bight—the marvellous, only genuwine Aus-
tralian Bight.
It was dug to float the best
Of the East unto the West,
And it’s floatin’ ’em unceasingly ’s the big Aus-
tralian Bight.
So those Easterners go westward mostly broke ;
They return with slugs and buy the worst of
wine ;
But the coasting skip.—contented sort of bloke !
Would rather trench that great big field of brine.
And he lifts old Breaksea Island with the sun,
And leaves the Bight next morning with regret,
For the older Indian Ocean and the run
Up to the Leu win ere the sun has set —
Leaves the Bight —the ample, generous and safe
Australian Bight.
If a bigger sort of gale
Laid her over to her rail,
She’s got the room to wear in on the big Australian
Bight.
Esperance, W.A.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
No drink in Hell. That’s where the tee-
totallers go.
Editor Bulletin, — Be old-age pensions.
It is true that outside cities there is no employ-
ment for elderly men. The free selectors never
employ such men, and the managers of bank-
owned stations simply dare not have an old man
on the place. Those of them who remain in the
bush begging are in the long run usually found
dead in the old huts. Ninety per cent, of the
armies of all countries are composed of men who
care not a bit for the country they serve under,
and would not be soldiers if they could obtain em-
ployment which would suit them better. The
scum of all cities and countries can be found in
the army. If those soldiers are entitled to pen-
sions, how much more so are the pioneers of our
goldfields and our back-country, the brave and
reckless army who have endured all sorts of
privations in developing resources of a country
wherein they only too often find themselves pau-
pers in the end. Blow, Australians, and let your
father die in the ditch !— Experience.


In Central Australia on the Finke River
the only clothes worn by a warrior of the Larapinta
tribe are a waistband and a necklet woven out of
his mother-in-law’s hair in token of her total subjec-
tion to him. And yet- Australian blacks are
accused of being men of low intelligence.
Chief Justice Madden attributes the deca-
dence of Egyptian civilisation to —
The corruption, vice, and loss of elevating principle
which ensued upon the abandonment of the ancient re-
verence of its people for their gods and their worship.
More confounding of effect with cause. Egypt
lost her reverence for her gods only when the good
gifts of the gods were monopolised by a few. She
went down because some citizens forgot that
others had citizen-rights. Split historical straws
as we may, the downfall of almost every civilisa-
tion can be explained by the aphorism: The
many had little, the few had much.
Premier Seddon is promoting a National
Option law whereby a teetotal majority, if such a
thing can be found in Maoriland, will have the
power to shut up every pub., and presumably
every brewery, in the three islands at one wild
swoop. And still the Cold Tea crowd is against
Seddon because he refuses to introduce a Prohibi-
tion Bill. That crowd desires two separate runs
for its Cold Tea. It wants National Option in the
first place, so that it can shut the pubs, if the popu-
lar majority is with it. And if the majority is
against it in the country it wants its majority—if
it can get one—in Parliament to pass a Prohibition
law in defiance of the majority outside. And as
Dick Seddon won’t help to make the required
majority in Parliament, he and the teetotal crowd
don’t drink amicably together these times.
Puncher : “ And, pray, who is your
correspondent ‘ Mahlstick,’ the superior per-
son who, having ‘been unable to tolerate Du
Maurier, ’ considers that the author of ‘ Trilby ’ is
‘no loss to the London Charivari ? ’ I can’t speak
of Du Maurier’s recent work, for I haven’t seen
half a dozen copies of Punch since 1883, but I can
remember the time when his drawings were in-
valuable to the paper. As an illustration of sas-
siety airs, graces, fans and crazes for the amuse-
ment of the British world in general, Du Maurier
had no rival for a long time. Moreover, he was
(one presumes) an original satirist, and a creator
of charming nursery humour. I take it that the
man who subsequently wrote ‘ Trilby ’ was the
author or polisher of most of the jokes he illus-
trated. Perhaps they were not sufficiently good
jokes to get toleration from ‘ Mahlstick,’ but to
me they seemed delicately pointed. Anyhow,
some of them have been sticking in my memory
these twenty years, during which long period I’ve
forgotten all the texts that I ever saw attached to
the work of his artist contemporaries, and every
drawing that illustrated them.”

To Sleep ! Perchance to Waite :
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sometimes the dull dream of the mono-
tonous, everyday round of a man’s life fades, and
the man wakes, lor one moment the present is
torn from him, and he is face to face with t
past. The days when he lived are with him again
for one agonising moment, ere the sleep and the
him as befoie.
m . * ,
There was a grizzled clerk in a lawyer s
office in sleepy Hobart. He had worked there
quietly and steadily for over twenty years, yet no
man, woman or child but knew him for the ex-
convict that he was. Phis did not trouble him,
except now and then, when he awoke, half-
blinded by the light of other days. To one man
only—his employer—was his whole story known.
* * * *
Love is no respecter of persons, and when
the articled clerk, working in dirty, picturesque
Dartford, fell in love with the only daughter of
one of the richest squires in Kent, the strength of
his passion made him feel that it must overthrow
every obstacle. The chief obstacle was the squire,
He was a fierce-tempered man ; he also held pro-
nounced ideas as to the total submission of a child
to its parent’s will, and the necessity of marrying
in one’s own station. To him there seemed little
difference between the penniless articled clerk and
any other ’prentice boy. He would see her in her
coffin, he told his daughter, rather than sanction
her marriage to one so much beneath her.
1 he girl trembled at Ins words, but, neverthe-
less her rides took her to the little dip on the
wide expanse of health, where she knew young
Richard Hamilton would be awaiting her. She
loved him intensely, though her dread of detection
grew to the terror of a nightmare.
One day she came with white lips.
“I can’t stay,” she said, “my father suspects
something. I know he does. He says he will
ride with me in future. O, Dick ! what shall we
do ? ”
Her kiss sent the burning blood surging through
him. He feared nothing as he comforted her and
watched her ride away.
* * * *
Each night, when the house was dark and
still, a silent figure stole down the stairs and
softly raised the iron bar of the library shutters.
Each night the ecstasy of meeting thrilled the
young lovers as in hushed voices they talked of
all that was coming, but sometimes, even while
he whispered confidently of the time when their
happiness would be complete, the girl would shiver
and turn pale at imaginary sounds.
.* * * . . *
One night, while he stood waiting outside
the closed shutter, a window opened softly above
him, and he caught his name like a sign in the
breeze.
“ Dick ! Dick ! I can’t come down. My father
is not in bed yet. Good-night, dear Dick, and go
away.”
“ Not without a kiss, if I break my neck over
it,” he whispered back, while he seized the thick
ivy-trunk in his hand. He was on the window-
siiJ, and for an ecstatic moment his arms were
round her. Then the door burst open, and the
hard face of the old squire, contorted with rage,
was before them. The young man could easily
have escaped in the same manner as he came, but
he was no coward, and his heart was full with the
innocence and strength of his love. He slipped
to his feet and stood upright. The old man with
an oath ordered his daughter from the room, and
in the look of suspicion and bitter hatred that
accompanied the words her lover read that which
seemed to make his heart stand still.
By the love he bore her, by the chivalry in his
young heart, he knew now that, whatever might
happen to him, for the honor of her name his lips
must be sealed.
In a dazed way he watched her as she crept away
in terror. He now felt stunned, unable to think
or speak. _
At the squire’s call the men-servants rushed in,
the nearest constable was summoned, and on a
charge of house-breaking the young man was fet-
tered and led away.
* * * *
It was very annoying to “my Lud ” that
the delivery of his nicely-rounded periods, em-
phasising tlie results of youthful depravity and
evil courses should be interrupted by a disturbance
at the far end of the court.
A girl, hatless and dishevelled, was struggling
with the guardian of the door, sobbing and trem-
bling with wild eyes fixed on the prisoner.
“ Dick ! ” she cried, “he locked me in. I tried
so to get out. O Dick, I broke through the win-
dow and fell.” She stretched out her little bleed-
ing hands, but the policeman’s grip was on her
arms. “Let me go to him,” she screamed, strugg-
ling wildly—“ I will go to him.” The Judge had
recovered from his surprise and spoke the last
words of the sentence. The girl ceased to struggle,
and the words echoed through a silent court.
“ Penal servitude for fourteen years.”
Then silence came again. The prisoner’s eyes
were fixed on the court-house door. His breath
shook him as it came and went. And then the
silence was broken. A loud shrill cry from a girl’s
lips filled the air, and was followed by peal upon
peal of mad laughter. The people shuddered as
they rose to their feet, but the laughter still went on
till the walls rang, and in the street it echoed
still, as the police carried out a hopeless maniac.
* » * *
For a space the floor seemed to shake
under the grey-haired man’s feet. He pressed his
hands to his head, while his whole existence
seemed to him one passionate cry for death and for
oblivion. For a moment. . . . “I want those
conveyances made out as soon as possible.”
“ Very good, sir,” said the clerk, as he took up
his pen once more.
MAMOOSE.
Assassinated during the century :—Czar
Paul, 1801; Sultan Selim the Third, ’OB ; Presi-
dent Kapodistias of Greece, ’3l; Duke Carl of
Parma, ’54 ; President Salnadeof Hayti, ’6O ; Lin-
coln, ’65 ; Prince Obenowich of Servia, ’6B ; Presi-
dent Merino of Ecuador, ’75 ; Sultan Abdul Aziz,
’75; Garfield, ’Bl ; Czar Alexander the Second,
’81; President Carnot, ’94 ; Shah of Persia, ’96.
Some years ago a certain struggling sub-
urban bank-clerk began to draw on the till to meet
his pony-racing losses. He was £9OO short when
the inspector loomed on the horizon. A day or
two later the Easter holidays also came and,
taking advantage of the four clear days ahead, the
man of subtractions decided upon a trip abroad
with as much ready cash as the safe then con-
tained. This resolve he carried into effect, and
was safe on the seas when the shortage was dis-
covered. Months passed and he gravitated to
London to play up the balance of his capital, to be
then recognised and transhipped to Australia,
-where three years’ hard awaited him. Released
by good conduct before the nominal expiration of
the sentence, the. erstwhile embezzler made his
way to Westralia, and getting into the Govt, ser-
vice under an assumed name became a highly-
esteemed officer. One day, however, his identity
became known, and next morning Mr.—“ Smith ”
—failed to appear at his accustomed desk. Coolgar-
die had just then broken out, and thither the re-
signed civil servant wended his way, picking up
enough money in promiscuous deals to take him to
London once again. At the big smoke three years
back anybody with practical Westralian experi-
ence held four aces, and after a six months’ sojourn
the erstwhile bank-clerk returned to the salty
city, his every pocket bulging with commissions.
He blossomed out in his old residence as the
representative of influential English syndicates,
and played his cards so well that not long ago lie
shook the dust of the Australias from his feet for
good, and started off to settle down in New York
or somewhere with ,£30,000. He is now firmly
convinced that honesty is the best policy, and
strongly ad vises any young man that floating com-
panies is a far better business than falsifying
accounts.
Pita.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“And now, Peter, my dear lad, do try and do
better. I am certain your father ”
“Step-father, mother.”
“Your step-father will relent and find some-
thing for you to do when you come back if only
you do well when you are away. Try to deserve
his good opinion, Peter; and, Peter, remember
Nellie Cameron. That girl loves you. Don’t
break her heart, my boy, and mine, too, by taking
up with any of those dreadful black women down
there. Now, there—good-bye—my dear —good-
bye.”
The intervals were motherly kisses. Mrs.
M‘Neil moved off wet-eyed. The girls, Peter’s
sisters, were laughing. They knew Peter. Also,
they enjoyed the covert glances of the sailors and
the wharf-loungers. They were well-fed, full-
blooded animals —like their affectionate brother
Peter. Presently they went ashore and joined
their mother. The steamer backed out and
steamed down Sydney Harbor.
Peter was a good fellow but a bad worker. He
had been bank-clerk, police-trooper, professional
footballer and billiard-marker. Now he was going
via Fiji to Raratonga with =650, his step-father’s
parting blast, and an appointment to boss a small
cotton-plantation.
Peter fell into the work capitally. It was good
fun bossing the docile Maoris. Enough rum and
gin for home consumption could always be ullaged
out of the stores, the living was good, there were
plenty of pigs, fowls, and fish, even if there was no
beel. And then there was Kuanini.
Kuanini was Pita’s Raratongan wife. Tall,
handsome, and full-figured, she had caught and
been caught by the stalwart young Sydneyite
before he had been a week on the, island. The
kikos had allowed him the usual fling after he took
Eossession, and then told him he would have to fix
is attention on one. Pita found out the alterna-
tive was a fine of ten dollars for every amour that
he might be detected in— and chose Kuanini.
His choice was good. She was a princess, and
regally resented any other woman’s admiration of
her rangatira pa-pa.
“Pita ! kahauri tu-tai t’ waihini,” and Pita, as
yet satisfied with his choice, forbore to speak to
any other woman.
But he wearied by-and-bye and went sailing
with Capt. Knuckleduster Johnson to Aitutaki,
an island 80 miles from Raratonga, famous for its
complaisant female population.
Johnson and Pita went ashore to find the most
uncompromising missionary that ever the society
sent out reigning the island. All transactions
must be in the market, and every sailorman, cap-
tain, passenger or deck-hand afloat before night-
fall. Ships must land no grog, and foreigners
parley no women under penalty of the calaboose.
Johnson knew all about this, so he sold cheap
and got all the women around him. What he
said m itters not, but late that night a boat landed
the captain and his passenger at a place where two
buxom Aitutaki girls stood waist-deep in the
water of the lagoon. Each taking her lover on her
back the pair walked backward over the sand of
the beach so that their tracks would confound the
vigilant kikos.
Now it happened that Johnson’s pilot was
Kuanini’s brother, and he, noticing Pita’s
nocturnal absence, quietly informed the vigilant
representative of the British Missionary Society.
This gentleman told the kikos, and the result the
next night was, as the two girls carried their live
loads back-stepping up the track, down swooped
the kikos armed with knives and clubs and cut
and pounded the Englishmen to a state of
insensibility. Johnson, wire-framed animal as he
was, recovered sufficiently to sail away in his
schooner. But Pita, handsome Pita, the women’s
pride, was inaeke—he would never be a sound man
again.
A steamer is landing her passengers at Rara-
tonga. They include those uncommon items,
white women. These are Mrs. N‘Neil and her
orphaned protege, Nellie Cameron.
They meet Maivenua—“ such a sweet girl,”
Miss Cameron says—a sentiment every male Euro-
pean visitor could endorse. “Ai, I tshow you
Pita.”
She leads them to the white-painted house and
enters. On the floor, in clean white suit, lies
Pita, his head on the lap of Kuanini, who has
been giving him some poi, ripe bananas mashed
with cocoa-nut juice.
His face meets his mother’s with a vacant gaze
and wanders to Miss Cameron’s. No gleam of
recognition is in his lustreless eyes. The kikos
that night on Aitutaki beach have taken away his
memory.
The white women are in tears. The big Maori
woman looks up—“ You no tak Pita? ”
“ No, my girl,” says Mrs. M‘Neil. And she did
not.

An Impossible Motion.
an
Sergeant William Sullivan is up to
something—he is up to the wall, upright
to the wall, right up to the upright wall.
It is a queer position for a real live ser-
geant ; it looks a painful one, but he does
not show his face—that would tell a tale.
He is laughing, although he is straining
every nerve to keep his equilibrium. It
is not because Sergeant Sullivan is a
giddy man that he makes this attempt to
lean sideways straight up to the wall.
Neither does he try it because there
is no convenient lamp - post to em-
brace. The fact is, he was offered
Five Pounds if he could do it, and, as
the picture shows, he attained the
desired position, but he didn’t attain the
desired Prize, for we were inquisi-
tive, and found out the Old Soldier
dodge. He had a nail on his finger—
an iron one—and driven into the wall.
That wasn’t in the piece, so Sergeant
Sullivan retired, and he says, “ You
can’t pull the breeches off a High-
lander, and you can’t do that- —it’s an
impossible motion. Now, readers, the
proprietor of Beecham’s Pills invites you
and all your friends to place your right
Side right up to an upright wall, every
portion of your side from ankle to the
shoulder being in contact with the wall.
Then attempt to raise the left foot from
the ground while the right side of the
body is still in contact with the wall. If
there is no fun to be got out of this
experiment, you are in a bad way, and
we feel it our bounden duty to remind
you that for ensuring buoyant spirits,
and the capacity to enjoy life to the
KT. WILLIAM SULLIVAN, full, BEECUAM’S PILLS are
Worth a Guinea a Box!
